Approval
This thesis has been prepared over five months at DTU Construct, Department of Mechanical
and Civil Engineering, at the Technical University of Denmark, DTU, in partial fulfillment for the
degree Master of Science in Civil Engineering, MSc Eng.
It is assumed that the reader has a basic knowledge in the areas of dynamics of structures,
CrossôÙLaminated Timber (CLT), floor vibration criteria and data processing.
The project has utilized AI to proofread, improve language, initialize and modify specific data
processing scripts.

Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
ii
==PAGE:4==
Abstract
A growing demand for adaptable buildings has led to increased use of open floor plans, necesôÙ
sitating longer floor spans that pose challenges in structural design, particularly in serviceability
limit states (SLS). In lightweight materials like crossôÙlaminated timber (CLT), floor vibrations
often govern the design, yet current guidelines neglect the influence of panelôÙtoôÙpanel connecôÙ
tions.
This study investigates the effect of buttôÙjoint connections and screw spacing on the modal propôÙ
erties and vibration response of CLT floors. The investigations are based on both experimental
and numerical methodologies to enhance understanding and illuminate the dynamic behavior
of the panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection in different floor configurations.
The experimental methodology consisted of random vibration tests analyzed with Operational
Modal Analysis (OMA) and resonance tests using a modal exciter. OMA provided a compreôÙ
hensive overview of the modal parameters, while the resonance tests were employed to obtain
more specific details.
The numerical methodology consisted of a deterministic finite element model updating (FEMU)
approach. It involved calibrating mechanical parameters of individual elements before assemôÙ
bling configurations and refining connection stiffness parameters. This was employed in a footôÙ
fall analysis.
Experimental testing revealed that while bending modes remained unaffected, torsion modes
exhibited a significant decrease in natural frequency as additional panels were connected, with
slight increases observed when decreasing the screw spacing. The damping ratio and vibraôÙ
tion response were influenced by the connection, though with varying influence across different
modes. The numerical investigation incorporated the calibrated connection stiffness, showing
that vertical stiffness was nearly rigid while rotational stiffness remained free. The modal propôÙ
erties of the connected panels showed a close resemblance to those of a monolithic floor. A
footfall analysis indicated a reduction in acceleration when increasing the number of connected
panels.
Overall, it is found that the buttôÙjoint connection between CLT panels does influence the modal
properties and vibration response of the CLT floor. The influence cannot be entirely attributed
to the connection itself, but rather to the changes in width, mass and stiffness that occur when
connecting the panels.
Keywords: CLT floor, panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection, buttôÙjoint, floor vibration criteria, deterministic
finite element model updating (FEMU)ëƒ experimental investigations, operational modal analysis
(OMA), pyOMA2

The completion of this work would not have been possible without the invaluable support and
guidance of numerous individuals and institutions. We are deeply grateful to those who conôÙ
tributed their time, expertise, and encouragement throughout this project.
First and foremost, we would like to express our sincere gratitude to Per og Lise Aarsleffs Fond
for their financial support to this project. This was especially valuable in regard to facilitating the
experiments.
Additionally, we are grateful to Rasmus Miller from Rothoblass for providing the necessary
screws used in the panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection of the CLT floor.
We wish to acknowledge the indispensable assistance provided by several individuals in faciliôÙ
tating the experimental setup and equipment. Our sincere thanks go to Kristian RiskûÎr Povlsen,
Head of Test Facilities, for providing laboratory space and offering valuable guidance on the test
setup. Special thanks are also owed to craftsman Claus Aagaard from the Test Facilities for his
significant contribution in constructing the experimental setup.
The successful execution of the experimental work required specialized equipment, and we are
immensely grateful to the following individuals at DTU. A profound thanks to Evangelos KatôÙ
sanos for supplying accelerometers and associated equipment critical to capturing the vibration
response of the CLT floor. In this regard, we also extend our gratitude to Benny Edelsten for his
guidance on the NIôÙFlexlogger data logging system and for providing a displacement sensor,
signal generator, and cables.
We would like to thank Rasmus Oxvang Mortensen for manufacturing the custom drilling guide,
a crucial tool designed specifically for this experiment. Our thanks also go to Troels Vestergaard
Kristensen for supplying a hygrometer, thermometer, and moisture meter, as well as to Morten
Berger Bentzen for manufacturing a base plate for the shaker.
Regarding the analysis of experimental data, we acknowledge the use of the OMA Toolbox for
MATLAB, developed by Rune Brincker and Carlos Ventura, as well as MATLAB scripts provided
by Sandro Diord Rescinho Amador during our earlier studies. We are grateful for these tools,
which facilitated the initial stages of our data analysis.
We would also like to extend our gratitude to Jonas Syders Knudsen from JSK Vibrations for his
invaluable guidance and insights on experimental methodologies. His practical expertise and
thoughtful advice are deeply appreciated.
Finally, we wish to express our profound gratitude to our supervisors, Jan Becker Hû¡gsberg and
Anders BjûÑrnfot. Their complementary expertise and dedicated support have been immensely
valuable. We are deeply thankful for their guidance and constructive feedback, which consisôÙ
tently directed us toward achieving the aims of this project.

==PAGE:19==
Part I
Problem Context and Theoretical Background
==PAGE:20==
1
Introduction
Building owners and architects see (and have seen) large opportunities in the construction of
adaptable buildings to futureôÙproof their investments. The adaptability enables buildings to be
transformed during their lifetime to embrace new occupants with new requirements. Open floor
plans have a significant impact on a buildingãs adaptability as they offer the necessary flexiôÙ
ble layoutôÙsolutions, however the structural grid increases accordingly, resulting in longer floor
spans. [1]
Longer floor spans introduce challenges in both ultimate limit state (ULS) and serviceability limit
state (SLS) design, as the floor panel thickness cannot be proportionally increased. These chalôÙ
lenges are particularly pronounced for lightweight floor materials with low damping properties,
such as crossôÙlaminated timber (CLT), where floor vibrations caused by footfall often govern the
design. [2] The design methods are based on one to four key verifications, while accounting
for different parameters [3]. The verifications are collectively characterized as floor vibration
criteria, which are generally based on a frequency limit and response criteria and are directly
connected to DS/ISO:10137, DS/ISO:2631ôÙ1, and DS/ISO:2631ôÙ2. These form the foundation
for the SLS vibration design of structures as they address how humans perceive and tolerate viôÙ
brations by evaluating parameters such as peak values, rootôÙmeanôÙsquare (RMS) accelerations
and velocities and the vibration dose value (VDV).
Figure 1.1: Open floor plan in CLT building in Australia [www.storaenso.com].
Designers, researchers and organizations have different approaches. [7] Arupãs design guide
for footfall induced vibrations of structures in [8] has been widely accepted by designers as it
is applicable for the majority of structures and materials. Its overall assessment and design
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
2
==PAGE:21==
procedure include an establishment of modal properties as the first step and a consideration of
modifying the structure as the last step if the floor doesnãt satisfy the vibration criteria.
For a CLT floor design, the modal properties and vibration response are generally analyzed
with the assumption of no connection between panels [9] or the floor acts monolithic. [10, 8, 11]
These assumptions and the influence of different panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections on the vibration
response have been numerically investigated in [12]. It is highlighted that the common pracôÙ
tice should be left behind and that the panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection shouldnãt be neglected in the
design. In [13], the influence of a panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection on the modal properties has been
experimentally determined. Additionally, a numerical model based on a probabilistic FEMU was
developed to represent and include the properties of a connection in a modal analysis. It highôÙ
lights a need for more research of other types of panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections in various floor
layouts.
Essentially, the vibration response of floors and occupant comfort are critical considerations,
often serving as governing factors in design. Current guidelines, handbooks and codes neglect
the influence of panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections when assessing the floor vibration criteria. LimôÙ
ited research exists on how the connections affect modal properties and vibration response.
Consequently, there is motivation in studying the impact of panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections in CLT
floors regarding dynamics. The buttôÙjoint connection, in particular, stands out due to the lack of
literature addressing its behavior under dynamic excitation. [13]
Figure 1.2: Illustration of a buttôÙjoint connection (adapted from [9]).
1.1
Problem Statement
The research questions of this project are:
1. How does the buttôÙjoint panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection and its screw spacing influence the
modal properties and vibration response of CLT floors?
2. How are finiteôÙelement models developed to assess and compare the modal properties
of a single CLT panel, multiple panels accounting for a panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection and
equivalent monolithic floors, and how do their vibration responses compare due to footfall?
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
3
==PAGE:22==
1.2
Delimitation
This project is limited to three crossôÙlaminated timber panels of 0.62x0.2x6 m each, which have
been supplied to an earlier project [14]. The SFSF boundary condition is utilized, which is
ensured by having no connection at the supports and no end restraints. The elements span
5.85 m oneôÙway. The modal properties are examined for a maximum frequency of 100 Hz. The
experimental methods are limited to estimating the modal properties, natural frequencies, mode
shapes, and damping ratio and vibration response by a modal exciter. The numerical method is
limited to natural frequencies and mode shapes and to foot analyses based on a single person
walking.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
4
==PAGE:23==
Overview of Theory
This chapter presents the most relevant theoretical concepts of the project. Initially, the chalôÙ
lenge of floor vibrations in relation to human comfort is described. Various guidelines for designôÙ
ing floors to mitigate this issue are introduced. Following, a presentation of the design principles
outlined in the Eurocode, with a particular focus on their applicability to timber constructions, is
given. Additionally, CLT floors are described concerning their material properties, stiffness, and
panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections.
To comprehensively understand the vibration behavior of CLT floors, the fundamental principles
of structural dynamics are specified. The MultiôÙDegreeôÙofôÙFreedom (MDOF) system is introôÙ
duced, along with its correlation to vibration response. Key modal properties, including natural
frequency, mode shapes, and damping ratio, are also presented.
Furthermore, this project employs the Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) method to investigate
the dynamic properties of CLT floors. Consequently, the general theoretical foundation of this
method is presented with particular emphasis on the principles behind Frequency Domain DeôÙ
composition (FDD) and Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI), which are used for analyzing
the measurement data.

==PAGE:24==
2
Cross Laminated Timber Floor
CrossôÙlaminated timber (CLT) is an engineered timber product which helped define a new class
of timber products known as mass timber. It was introduced in midôÙ1990ãs in Austria and GerôÙ
many, where it gained popularity in residential and nonôÙresidential buildings. In the 21st century,
it has gained more interest due to the increasing focus on sustainability.
Figure 2.1: Construction site of Marmormolen from October, 2023. (Rambû¡ll Lecture at DTU,
2024)
It is a relatively lighter material than other primary structure materials such as concrete and
masonry. This results in less demands for the foundation, easy handling on site and along
with a high level of prefabricationëƒ it facilitates a rapid project completion. It has good thermal
insulation, sound insulation and performance under fire, which is highly desirable. CLT panels
consist of multiple layers of lumber, which orthogonally alternates from layer to layer. The most
common layer compositions are based on an uneven number of layers to define a main and
secondary span direction. The CLT panels are used as wall and floor elements. [10]
Figure 2.2 display an exploded view of the CLT builtôÙup, with the individual lamellas and orthogôÙ
onal layers.
2.1
Understanding of Human Response to Floor Vibration
Floor vibration is generally characterized and designed for dynamic loading. The governing
dynamic load is typically people walking [2], which is characterized as ãfootfallã in general terms.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
6
==PAGE:25==
Figure 2.2: Exploded view of CLT builtôÙup [15]
This includes walking, running and crowd loads. The load from each step acts as an exciter
and it forces the floor to vibrate. Different footfall time histories can be seen in Figure 2.3.
Figure 2.3: Example of footfall time histories from Wilford and Young in [8].
The floorãs vibration displacement is typically not significant to the human eye nor significant in
terms of the structural integrity. However, the vibrations are felt by the occupants and it has a
negative impact on the comfort of occupants and how the quality of a floor is perceived. [8]
Many factors influence the human response, tolerance and view of comfort due to vibrations as
our tolerance changes according to direct and indirect effects. ãDirect effects include the freôÙ
quencies, magnitude, duration, variability, form, directions of the vibration and intervals between
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
7
==PAGE:26==
vibration events, or exposure of the human subjects to the vibration. Indirect effects on the subôÙ
jective response to an environment that includes vibrations are: audible noise and infraôÙsound,
visual cues, population type, familiarity with vibration, structural appearance, confidence in a
building structure, height above ground, warning of events, activities engaged in, knowledge of
the source of vibration, etc.ã ôÙ according to DS/ISO:10137
The average threshold of human perception is defined by the base curve: the threshold is
defined by a rootôÙmeanôÙsquare acceleration m/s2 in the frequency domain. This is because
people perceive vibrations differently to specific frequencies. The most sensitive frequency
range for a human is between 4ôÙ8 Hz, where the accelerations are constant. For frequencies
greater than 8 Hz, humans have a linear increasing tolerance to the acceleration, which equals
a constant velocity within the frequency domain.
Figure 2.4: Building vibration ( a [rms] ) for zôÙaxis base curve for acceleration, Annex C, Figure
C.1 in DS/ISO:10137
The average threshold of human perception for the base curve (zôÙaxis) is therefore

methods to ensure the floor vibration criteria are fulfilled. Both are introduced in the following

Design Guides
Floor vibration design guides include different design methods and have been developed for
different materials by different publishers in order for a design to fulfill the human perception of
comfort. This section includes short descriptions of the following design guidesã method
ãÂ Wood: CLT Handbook (Canada), 2019, publisher ãFPInnovationsã [10]
ãÂ General: Arupãs Design Guide, 2006, publisher ãThe Concrete Centerã [8]

whose criteria are based on the work of Hamm and Richter, 2009 [17] and Augustin, 2012 [18].

2.2.2
Arupãs Design Guide
The general and widely accepted design guide for floor vibration due to footfall for any material
is Arupãs Design Guide in [8]. [12, 20] The general method can be expressed as a stepwise
procedureëƒ Fig. 4.1 in [8]. This consists of one of the two response analysesëƒ a steady state
resonant response analysis or a transient response analysis. The relevant analysis is chosen,
based on a frequency limit for the lowest eigenfrequency of a floor. The guide includes analysis
of simple practical examples determined with handôÙcalculations for prediction of footfall induced
vibration for floors in general. The methodology is based on the paper of Wilford and Young,
2005 [21], which included Kerr, 1998 [22] as their principal source of data.

number of layers, which are alternating orthogonally. This results in a major and minor span
direction. The lamella is made from softwood of CôÙ or TôÙclass. The strength class is typically
specified as the same for all layers, but strength classes can differ for each layer to enhance
certain properties. [9] The properties for the lamellas are given in EN 338. The strength paramôÙ
eter for CLT (Table 2.3) is based on the lamella properties. The subscript x, y and z relate a

The bending stiffness, EI, is an important parameter in determining the fundamental frequency
according to Eq. 2.7 and in general important for the floor vibration response. For CLT, the
shear deformation of the transverse layers should be taken into account. The analytical methôÙ
ods of Gamma, Extended Gamma and Timoshenko are presented and examples are given for
determining an effective bending stiffness, seen in [9]. There are other methods, e.g. the shear
analogy/Kreuzinger method defined in Appendix A in Canadian CLT Handbook. Appendix A
in this project gives an overview of the assumptions and limitation to calculations methods in
general according to [28].
Other parameters also influence floor vibrations.
By altering one of the three fundamental
propertiesãmass, damping and stiffnessãof the equation of motion (EOM), introduced in chapôÙ
ter 3, the vibration response is affected. Numerous studies have been conducted to investigate
how various parameters influence these properties and, consequently, the vibration response.
A selection of state of the art research will be presented below to provide an overview of the
recent research made for floor vibrations.
General
Weckendorf, Ussher, and Smith, 2016 [29] experimentally investigated multiple variablesã inôÙ
fluence on eigenfrequencies and damping ratio of CLT floors. The variables consisted of plan
aspect ratio, support conditions and materials, clamping effects, CLT type and peopleãs position
affect on damping.
Damping
The damping of civil structures is typically enhanced by external dampers. For CLT floors,
Huang and Chang, 2018 [30] and Jiang et al., 2024 [31] numerically investigated the innovative
shapeôÙmemory alloy tuned mass damper, SMAôÙTMD, effect on vibration response. JarnerûÑ,
Brandt, and Olsson, 2015 [32] tested the damping ratio for simple supported floor panels with
an interlayer of Sylodynôˋ , a soundôÙproofing product, by laboratory tests and inôÙsitu tests at difôÙ
ferent construction stages. The inôÙsitu conditions had a considerable influence on the damping.
Additionally, a major change in natural frequencies was found for the coupling of elements and
placement of partition walls.
Stiffness
Ussher et al., 2024 [33] investigated the effect of partition walls on the vibration serviceability of
CLT floors. The presense of partitions can improve the vibration comfort of timber floors due to
a ãstiffening effectã leading to significant increase in the first fundamental frequency.
Zhang and Chui, 2024 [34] experimentally investigated the end restraint boundary conditions
from top loads, selfôÙtapping screws and steel angle brackets for changes in fundamental natuôÙ
ral frequencies and mid span deflection and introduced an end fixity factor to account for the
rotational stiffness from the end restraint in natural frequency.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
15
==PAGE:34==
Stieb et al., 2023 [35] experimentally investigated multiple edge connections with woodôÙbased
panels with inclined screws or screw press gluing, inclined screws only, timberôÙconcrete comôÙ
posite (TCC) solution and serrated steel plates in fourôÙpoint bending test (4PBT) to build twoôÙ
way spanning CLT floors. Especially TCC and the serrated steel plates were characterized by
distinct ductile failure behavior, which yielded subject for additional research.
EdgeôÙ, interôÙpanelôÙ and panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection are different words referring to the same.
Milojeviá et al., 2023 [12] numerically investigated the influence of panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection
(both halfôÙlap joint and single surface spline) on vibration response of CLT floors due to pedestrianôÙ
induced loading. The joints were modelled as equivalent elastic strips according to Paolini et al.,
2017 [36]. The rotational stiffness of the halfôÙlap joint was based on Macpherson et al., 2018
[37], who defined a simplified calculation for the rotational stiffness of the halfôÙlap joint and veriôÙ
fied the calculation with a 4PBT.
For a 6x6 m CLT floor consisting of two panels with a SFSF boundary condition and walking
path 1 Milojeviá et al., 2023 [12] found an increase in the rootôÙmeanôÙsquare (RMS) acceleration
for both joints at 10% and 11%, respectively, and 80% for the situation with no connection beôÙ
tween the panels when related to a monolithic panel.
Macpherson et al., 2018 [37] carried out a static 4PBT with bending parallel to the interôÙpanel
connection line. This results in a high engagement of the joint and bending stiffness. Their reôÙ
sult may therefore not be utilized completely as the stiffness of the connection can be different
under dynamic loading due to small strains involved. This was also stated by Milojeviá et al.
2023, and again in Milojeviá et al. [13]. Milojeviá et al. 2024 [13] experimentally and numerically
investigated a halfôÙlap joint between two and three panels based on the elastic strip method
from Paolini et al., 2017 [36]. The connection properties was calibrated with an extensive probôÙ
abilistic FEMU. The study yielded that the rotational stiffness from static 4PBTs from rare studies
cannot be utilized reliably in vibration response analysis. Additionally, future research should
be extended to other types of interôÙpanel connections, e.g. buttôÙjoint.
It was seen from the current state of the art and other recent stateôÙofôÙtheôÙart [38] that there is a
lack of research regarding the panelôÙtoôÙpanel connectionãs effect on the modal properties and
vibration response.
PanelôÙtoôÙPanel Connection
The panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections are only verified in ULS scenarios. It transfers the wind loading
from the facade to the stabilizing walls and secures uneven imposed loading to not result in
uneven deflection of two adjacent panels. They are therefore verified in ULS [9] and not taken
into account in SLS and the vibration serviceability criteria. [27, 11, 10]
Simple examples for the ULS verifications can be seen in [9] along with the bearing capacities for
a screw pair depending on the direction in eq. (4.51)ôÙ(4ôÙ53). The connection stiffness were seen
in [12, 13] to influence the modal properties and vibration response significantly. It is therefore
relevant to get an approximate idea of the initial magnitude of these most relevant stiffness.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
16
==PAGE:35==

3
Dynamics of Structures
This chapter is based on the theory described by [41] regarding dynamics of structures. The
dynamic behavior of a CLT floor is complex as it involves simultaneous motion of several masses
in shapes that are not known before the analysis. To be able to analyze the modal properties
and vibration response of a CLT floor, it has to be considered as a multi degree of freedom
(MDOF) system.
3.1
MDOF: Free Undamped Vibration
The dynamic properties of a system can initially be characterized by its free vibration characterôÙ
istics, natural frequency and mode shape. The natural frequency (or natural vibration frequenôÙ
cies) are the frequencies a structure is susceptible to resonate at. Mode shapes define the load
distributions that results in excitation of these resonances.
A system exposed to a timeôÙdependent load requires the time variation and load distribution
across the structure to be taken into account to be able to fully characterize the vibration reôÙ
sponse. In addition, the damping characteristics of the structure needs to be included.
Initially, the free undamped vibrations of a linear system consisting of multi degree of freedom
with n number of degrees of freedom is introduced. Free undamped vibrations mean that the
vibrations are governed by the equation of motion (EOM), where there is no loading or damping,
C, yielding
Mô´q + Kq = 0
(3.1)
where M and K are (nôÙbyôÙn)ôÙmatrices containing the information of the mass and stiffness deôÙ
scribing the structure, respectively. q(t) = [q1(t), q2(t), ..., qn(t)] describes the time dependent
displacements of the system. A dot denotes differentiation with respect to time t.
A solution in the form of a harmonic oscillation with constant shape is sought for. The time
variation is expressed in a complex notation, which results in

3.2.3
Logarithmic Decrement
If a SDOF system is excited as resonance by an excitation force, the vibration response will
reach a steady state. If the excitation force is terminated at a point in time, the free decay of
the response is seen. This means that the amplitude of the structure will slowly decay. The
logarithmic decrement is found as
öÇn = ln
( ak
ak+n
)
(3.30)

where ak is the value of a chosen peak in the free decay, ak+n is the value of the next peak,
which is n number of periods away from ak. With n as a variable, the damping ratio can be
determined as
öÑ = öÇn
2ün
(3.31)
Types of damping
Friction damping takes the damping arising from friction between layers interacting with each
other when the structure is vibrating into account . For a CLT floor, this can among other be the
friction between the lamellas, the friction in the material or the friction in the connection of the
connected panels or supports.
Viscous damping is damping that is proportional to the velocity of the system. This means that
the faster the mass is moving, the more damping force is resisting that motion.
Fluid damping occurs due to the structure moving through or interacting with surrounding fluid
such as air, water or oil.
3.3
MDOF: Damped Vibration
Assessing the full vibration response of the earlier described MDOF system, it can be described
by the set of second order differential equations
Mô´q + C ùq + Kq = f(t)
(3.32)
where f(t) is the load history, and C is the viscous damping matrix of the system. To solve the
dynamic response problem, two sets of initial conditions are required:
q(0) = q0
ùq(0) = ùq0
(3.33)

and then by preôÙmultiplying with UT , the following is obtained
(UT MU)ô´r(t) + (UT CU)ùr(t) + (UT KU)r(t) = UT f(t)
(3.34)

damping matrix to be diagonal gives c = UT CU.
Now the full equation of motion is uncoupled in n independent equations given as

These independent equations each represent a SDOF system of the MDOF system and can
therefore be solved as a SDOF system.

4
Operational Modal Analysis
Operational Modal Analysis or OMA is a method to estimate modal properties of a structure/sysôÙ
tem by analyzing the response of the structure when undergoing ambient vibrations or ãinôÙ
operation conditionsã. The main properties, which are normally estimated are natural frequenôÙ
cies and damping factors of the system. Additionally, the behavior of the structure in the form
of mode shapes associated to the natural frequencies can be studied.[42]
The fundamental concept of OMA is to excite the system with characteristics similar to white
noise or random noise. A critical aspect of the excitation force is that its energy must span a
broad frequency range. This can ensure that all modal characteristics of the system are excited
and can be captured through appropriate measurements.
Moreover, OMA has gained widespread popularity because it requires only the systemãs reôÙ
sponse, rather than the input load used for excitation. This is in contrast to Experimental Modal
Analysis (EMA), which uses controlled impact hammers, shakers and other controlled input
methods. OMA typically employs excitation methods such as scratching devices and random
signals, among others. [42]
4.1
pyOMA2
An open source OMA toolbo, which is called pyOMA2 [43] can be utilized to process and analyze
measured acceleration signals utilizing different algorithms. pyOMA2 allows use of the concept
of roving and reference sensors which makes it possible to have more data points that can
be combined to better identify natural frequencies and mode shapes and associated damping
ratios. Additionally, the ability to visualize the mode shapes with pyOMA2 is a valuable feature.
The structure of pyOMA2 follows the workflow in the figure below
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
26
==PAGE:45==
Figure 4.1: The work flow of pyOMA2.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
27
==PAGE:46==
First, it is determined, whether the data measurements correspond to a single or multiôÙsetup
configuration. Subsequently, the estimation of modal properties is performed using various
algorithms. Finally, the results are presented and the mode shapes are visualized through
graphical representations.
4.2
Operational Response to Modal Parameter Estimation
In this project, the multiôÙsetup (MS) PreGER has been utilized and the algorithms, frequencyôÙ
domain decomposition, (FDD), and covarianceôÙbased stochastic subspace identification, (SSIôÙ
COV), have been used to estimate the modal properties. The algorithms are based on a freôÙ
quency domain method and a time domain method, respectively.
4.2.1
FDD
The FDD is based on the peakôÙpicking technique, which relies on the principle that systems
exhibit stronger responses when excited near their natural frequencies. The principle converts
measured signals in the timeôÙdomain to the frequencyôÙdomain using Fast Fourier Transform
(FFT) for spectral analysis. The application of signal autoôÙcorrelation before applying FFT yields
Power Spectral Densities (PSD), which indicate the distribution of signal power across frequenôÙ
cies. The natural frequencies are identified as prominent peaks in the PSD, corresponding to
frequencies where the systemãs response is strongest.
The FDD takes the ãpeakôÙpickingã technique one step further. It takes the signals and converts
it into spectral density matrices (SD) by using FFT on the autoôÙ and cross correlation of the
signal. Then it performs a singular value decomposition (SVD) of the spectral density matrices.
Therefore, the SVD concentrates information from all spectral density functions. This gives a
higher accuracy when estimating modal parameters even when having closely spaced modes
or a lot of noise in the signal. [42]
The FDD extends the peakôÙpicking technique by enhancing its accuracy. Using both autoôÙ and
crossôÙcorrelation of the signals, it transforms the measured signals into spectral density (SD) maôÙ
trices through FFT. Subsequently, a Singular Value Decomposition (SVD) is performed on the
SD matrices, effectively consolidating information from all spectral density functions. This apôÙ
proach improves the estimation of modal parameters, particularly in cases with closely spaced
modes or significant noise in the signal.[42]
The most important aspects of FDD are explained in more detail below including FFT, SD and
SVD.
FFT
A signal is processed from the timeôÙdomain to the frequencyôÙdomain by the Discrete Fourier
Transformation (DFT) and it is essential to understand FFT, as it is an effective algorithm to
compute the DFT of a signal.
DFT can be explained as a correlating signal that is used to rotate the signal from the timeôÙ
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
28
==PAGE:47==
domain to the frequency domain
Xk =
Nã1
ã
n=0
xnei2ü k
N n
ãXk = xnR
(4.1)
where Xk is the frequency domain signal at frequency k, where each frequency k contains the
sum of the time signal. xn is the discrete time domain signal in sample number n. N is the
total number of samples and ei2ü k
N n is known as the correlating signal. In matrix notation the
correlating signal can be written as R which is an NxN matrix. Each column corresponds to
the sines and cosines associated with frequency k. [44]
The aboveôÙmetioned operation can become computationally costly for a signal of many samples.
The FFT algorithm can be applied in this case, as it takes advantage of different symmetries
in the matrix R. This means, that instead of duplicating many of the calculations, which the
DFT would, FFT reduces the number of operations by populating one answer to a calculation
in multiple locations. [42]
A figuratively explanation of DFT can be seen in the figure below. The different (co)sine waves,
which are present in the time signal can be depicted and displayed in a frequency domain.
Figure 4.2: Schematics of the DFT.
The sampling frequency fs is an important parameter in determining the frequency range in
which the modal properties can be estimated. In DFT, the timeôÙsignals can be converted into
the frequency range from 0 up to the Nyquist frequency fv given by
fv = fs
2
(4.2)
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
29
==PAGE:48==
The number of sample points in the frequency domain is half the number of those in the time
domain.
FFT is not applied on unfiltered raw data signals as it typically includes noise, trends etc. FilterôÙ
ing of the raw signals can therefore yield more accurate results.
Decimation (or downôÙsampling) is a method often used when the sampling frequency is higher
than actually needed, so a reduction in the number of data points is applied . It advantages are
that the computational time is reduced and it helps removing unnecessary highôÙfrequency noise
or components. [42]
Detrending removes trends e.g. longôÙterm movements or patterns, which are in the data set.
By removing these trend the analysis can focus on e.g. shortôÙterm deviations, patterns or corôÙ
relations. It removes DC offsets (=the mean value of the waveform) in the signals so the signal
is forced to have a zero mean. [42]
SVD
It is described stepwise how the correlograms method estimates the SDôÙmatrices followed by
the SVD.
1. Autocorrelation and cross correlation function of the signal
Patterns and periodicity of a signal can be quantified by relating the signal to itself but timeôÙ
shifted. This is performed by the autocorrelation function.
The crossôÙcorrelation measures the similarity between two different signals as one is timeôÙ
shifted relative to the other, which is useful for finding dependencies between two or multiple
signals over time. FFT of the crossôÙcorrelation estimates the SD, where the SVD can be perôÙ
formed.
The autocorrelation function is defined as
Rx(ü) = E[x(t)x(t + ü)]
(4.3)
where x(t) is the signal at time t and ü is the lag (delay) time. E is the expectation operator.
The expectation operator helps to ãaverage outã the correlation, filtering out noise or random
fluctuations, and provides a clearer view of the underlying pattern in the time series data.
The cross correlation is defined by generalizing the autocorrelation for two signals e.g. meaôÙ
surements from two different sensors recording the response of a system.
Rxy(ü) = E[x(t)y(t + ü)]
Ryx(ü) = E[y(t)x(t + ü)]
(4.4)
In case of multiple measurements N, they are arranged in a response vector y(t) = y1(t), y2(t), ..., yN(t)T .
Here the correlation function CF covering both auto and cross correlation can be used
R(ü) = E[y(t)yT (t + ü)]
(4.5)
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
30
==PAGE:49==
The diagonal elements are autocorrelation functions, and offôÙdiagonal elements are crossôÙcorrelation
functions. [42]
2. Windowing
Windowing divides the truncated signal into smaller, manageable subsets, or ãwindowsã, for
more efficient processing and analysis. The purpose of windowing is to balance resolution and
leakage. Different windows can be applied e.g. a rectangular or Hamming window depending
on the signal and desired outcome of the analysis.
3. Spectral Density
Applying the FFT to the windowed signal the FFT results in the Spectral Density.
S(f) = F
(
R(ü)
)
(4.6)
where F is the FFT.
4. SVD Essentially, the Singular Value Decomposition (SVD) is a method to factorize a matrix
into three components. This is utilized on the spectral density.
Any matrix A can be unconditionally decomposed into
A = UöÈVT
(4.7)
where öÈ is a rectangular diagonal matrix, U and V are orthogonal matrices. [45]
ãÂ öÈ contains the singular values of A in its diagonal in descending order.
ãÂ U contains the normalized eigenvectors of AAT in descending order of their eigenvalues.
ãÂ V contains the normalized eigenvectors of AT A in descending order of their eigenvalues.

4.2.2
SSI
Stochastic Subspace Identification (SSI) can estimate the systems modal properties by analyzôÙ
ing correlations in the time domain. The overall principles of SSI is initially described and a
more detailed description of the principles of the covarianceôÙbased SSI (SSIôÙCOV) is given.
SSI is a method, where a parametric model is fitted directly to the raw times series data. An
optimization of the model parameters is performed in order for the predicted system response
and the actual measured system response are as close as possible. For a discrete times series
in general, the model can be formulated as a discreteôÙtime stochastic stateôÙspace model
xn+1 = Axn + wn
(4.8)
yn = Dxn + vn
(4.9)
where xn is the state vector, yn is the sampled output vector, A is the discrete state matrix,
D is the discrete output matrix, the process noise wn contains the disturbances and model
inaccuracies and the measurement noise vn contains the noise of the measurement from the
sensor.
The first equation is generally called the state equation and contains the information of the
systemãs dynamic response. The second equation is the observation equation and contains the
information on the part of the systemãs dynamic response that can be observed in the modelãs
output. [46]
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
32
==PAGE:51==

is given as a sample shift k. This is related by k = ãtfs where fs is the sampling frequency.

[R]i+1
ôñ ôñ ôñ
[R]2i+1
ÿÈ¿
ÿÈ¤ÿÈ¤ÿÈ¤ÿÈ¤ÿÈ¤ÿÈ£
(4.11)
This matrix contains subôÙmatrices corresponding to the different correlation matrices between
all measurement channels. 2i will be the maximum number of time lags included and i is the
number of block rows that are included in the Hankel matrix.
Next step in the process is to deconstruct the blockôÙHankel matrix into observability matrices,
[Oi] and controllability matrices [Ci]
[H]i = [Oi][Ci]
(4.12)
In the observability matrix, [Oi], [A], the discrete state matrix, and [D], the discrete output matrix,
are contained. Further analysis of [Oi] will yield eigenvalue decomposition of [A] and hereby
the eigenvalues and eigenvectors of the system can be estimated. [46]
Block rows
In the Hankel matrix the number of block rows to include has to be manually specified. The
number of block rows will have an influence on the rest of the analysis. If too few rows are
included it can be hard to depict the dynamical properties and find patterns in the signal. It is
better to have more block rows as more time lags are included and therefore a better estimation
can be done, however more noise can also be included.
Order
The specified order also has an influence on the modal parameter estimation and is used in
the truncation. The truncation represents an approximation due to noise and imperfections in
the system and the order corresponds to a level of truncation. Too low of an order can result
in an incomplete model and therefore missing modal parameters. Too high of an order can
include spurious modes. In order to be able to distinguish spurious poles from physical polesëƒ
a stabilization diagram is typically utilized.
Stabilization diagram
In the stabilization diagram the estimated natural frequencies can be depicted as the it contains
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
33
==PAGE:52==
the stable and unstable poles. An example of this is seen in Figure 4.4. The green dots repreôÙ
sent stable poles, while the red dots represent unstable ones. A stable pole remains aligned
as the model order increases, signifying a physical mode that should be included in modal
parameter estimation. In contrast, spurious poles appear scattered beyond a specified threshôÙ
old, indicating instability. This threshold, defined by the analyst, varies for natural frequencies,
damping ratios, and mode shapes. In a stabilization diagram, physical poles can start at both
low and high model orders, depending on the excitation level of the modes. [42]
Figure 4.4: Example of a stabilization chart from pyOMA2. [43]
4.2.3
PreGER
PreôÙGlobal Estimation ReôÙscaling (PreGER) is a method to rescale and combine data from
a multiôÙsetup with one of more reference sensor and roving sensors. The reference sensor
is fixed at the same location in all tests while the roving sensors can be moved around the
system in order to measure different positionsã response. PreGER are able to reôÙscale the raw
measurements before creating a global dataset. It normalizes the data to a common reference,
allowing differences in excitation force across tests to be disregarded. This ensures consistency
and alignment of datasets for the global analysis and later OMA. [47]
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors

Overview of Methodology
As many experiments have been conducted and Finite Element Modeling (FEM) has been utiôÙ
lized throughout this project, Figure 1 and Figure 2 provide an overview. These figures illustrate
the methodology applied in the project and demonstrate how the different approaches compleôÙ
ment each other in addressing the research questions.
Initially, experimental tests were conducted in the laboratory to estimate the modal properties of
the CLT floor with a particular focus on the damping ratio, which cannot be determined through
FEM. The modal properties and vibration response have been analyzed for both a single CLT
panel and multiple CLT panels connected by a buttôÙjoint connection with varying screw spacing.
Figure 1: Schematic overview of the experimental methodology explained stepwise by the numôÙ
bering.
The methodology was based on an extensive preliminary work and background investigation.
This included designing and constructing the experimental setup, acquiring and testing the necôÙ
essary equipment and evaluating various experimental methods to ensure reliable results. A
detailed description of this process can be found in Appendix B. To compare the modal propôÙ
erties of a single panel, connected panels and an equivalent monolithic CLT floor, FEM was
employed.
See next page for FEM overview
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
36
==PAGE:55==
The Finite Element Modelling (FEM) is based on a deterministic approach. It utilizes experimenôÙ
tally determined modal properties to calibrate parameters through sensitivity analyses, ensuring
accurate modeling of individual panels, connected panels and variations in screw spacing. The
results were compared to those of equivalent monolithic panels and later used in a footfall analôÙ
ysis to evaluate the vibration response of the floors.
Figure 2: Schematic overview of the FEM methodology explained stepwise by the numbering.

Experimental and Analytical Methods
In this project, experiments have been conducted to study the problem statements. The purôÙ
pose of the experimental investigation is to estimate the stiffness, different modal parameters
and vibration response of CLT panels. The Random Vibration test and Resonance Tests are conôÙ
ducted for a single CLT panel, two connected CLT panels and three connected CLT panels. The
panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection, which is utilized, is the buttôÙjoint. Additionally, the aboveôÙmentioned
tests are carried out for varying spacing between the screw pairs in the buttôÙjoint connection.
These are the centerôÙtoôÙcenter (c/c) distances between the screw pairs.
ãThe parameters mentioned above are estimated by performing the following tests:ã
ãÂ General Testing: Measurements of CLT elementsã dimension, mass, water content, and
the room temperature and humidity
ãÂ Static Deflection: A static deflection test to calculate the bending stiffness EI of the CLT
panels
ãÂ Random Vibration with MultiôÙsetup: A test was conducted by scratching the surface of the
CLT panel(s) to estimate natural frequencies below 100 Hz, along with their corresponding
mode shapes and damping ratios, using Operational Modal Analysis
ãÂ Resonance Tests for the first and second bending mode and the first torsion mode
ã Sweep: The CLT panel(s) is excited with a shaker by sweeping through different
frequencies to find a specific natural frequency
ã Acceleration: The CLT panel(s) is excited at resonance with a shaker to estimate the
vibration response of the floor
ã Damping Ratio: The CLT panel(s) is excited at resonance with a shaker and the free
decay after termination of the excitation is measured to estimate the damping ratio

1.1
Experimental Setup, Equipment and Design
1.1.1
Location
All tests were performed at the Technical University of Denmark in Building 119, 2800 Lyngby,
Room 936 ãFlex Labã. The room is roughly 8x16 m with a concrete floor. A small drain is situated
in the middle of the room. There is therefore a small inclination towards this drain in the floor,
but only for a small proximity ã1 m. This should not influence the experimental setup.
1.1.2
Base Setup
The three CLT panels, utilized in the experimental investigations, are from the producer, BinderôÙ
holz Brettsperrholz. They are named BBS XL 200 with the performance stated in Appendix E
and [48], [49]. Each panel is made up of five 40 mm thick lamellas of C24 softwood in both the
longitudinal (3) and transverse layers (2). The total dimensions are 200x620x6000 mm and they
have a given density of 480 kg/m3. The panels were sponsored for an earlier project JûÎger
and Jacobsen, 2021 [14].
The experimental setup involves a base configuration of the panels and their supporting strucôÙ
ture. The panels are supported by two UNPôÙ200 steel beams, so they are considered to be
simply supported with an effective span of 5850 mm. Specifically, the panels rest on the top
flange of the beams, and the profiles are positioned so that the distance between the two webs
corresponds to the effective span length. The UNPôÙ200 beams have a total height of 200 mm,
a flange width of 75 mm, a flange thickness of 11.5 mm, and a web thickness of 8.5 mm. The
stability of the steel beams is ensured by connecting two 8x8 lumber beams (200x200 mm) beôÙ
tween the steel beams to form a hybrid frame. Each connection is made with two french wood
screws.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
40
==PAGE:59==
Note that the lumber was not long enough to span the full 5850 mm, so a timberôÙtoôÙtimber joint
with a single shear plane, reinforced with a plywood board, is used to ensure that the frame acts
as a unified structure. It is assumed that the natural frequencies of the supporting structure will
not interfere with the expected natural frequencies of the floor, as the UNPôÙ200 steel beams
become stiffer due to the addition of the 8x8 lumber. The base setup can be seen in Figure 1.8
below.
(a) Base setup from the side. 3 CLT panels being supported in each end by the hybrid frame.
(b) Base setup seen from the left end in relation
to (a). Panel 1 in the middle, Panel 2 to the left,

1.1.3
Connection
The two or three connected CLT panels are connected by x number of buttôÙjoints at a 45ãÎangle
to the surface of the CLT panel. The buttôÙjoint connection can be seen in Figure 1.2, in which
the relevant distances of the connection are seen. These were determined based on industrial
recommendation, research articles and minimum distances from data sheet Appendix E.2. The
specific screw type is ãVGZ 9x220mm Full threaded screw with cylindrical headã with a sharp
1 cut tip sponsored by Rothoblaas [40]. The screw pair spacing ranges from 3000ôÙ300 mm for
two elements (2E) and 3000ôÙ1200 mm for three elements (3E).
Figure 1.2: ButtôÙjoint connection between two CLT panels. The screw pair seen from the surface
of the CLT panel.
To connect the two (and later three) CLT panels in practice, two rounds of predrilling were
necessary due to the design of the specially manufactured jig. First, the screw placement is
marked on the surface of the CLT panels. Next, preôÙdrilling through the first CLT panel relative
to the screw entry point is carried out using a û5 mm long auger bit and the jig to ensure a 45ô¯
angle. The jig is held against the edge of the CLT panel while drilling. This process is illustrated
in Figure 1.3a.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
42
==PAGE:61==
After all initial holes have been preôÙdrilled in both CLT panels, two large bar clamps are attached
around the first two CLT panels at each support. The panels are then brought together by
tightening the clamps, as they slide on the UNPôÙprofile supportsã top flange. Once the clamps
can no longer be tightened and the CLT panels are in contact, the second round of preôÙdrilling
takes place. The already preôÙdrilled holes are utilized and the long auger bit is employed again
for preôÙdrilling in the second member. A countersink drill is used to create a countersink hole
for the screw head. This ensures that the screw heads will sit flush or under the surface of the
CLT panel. The number of screwedôÙin screws depends on the performed test.
Some of the steps can be seen in Figure 1.3.

NIôÙFlexLogger is a data logging software, which can acquire, visualize and log mixed measureôÙ

The data processing is made in the programming languages of MATLAB and Python. For the

predecessor, PyOMA (from [51]). All other data processing is made in MATLAB. The scripts

1.3
Static Unit Point Load Deflection
The investigation of the static unit point load deflection is performed to later determine the apôÙ
parent bending stiffness of each element and compare the result to the calculation methods of
both Gamma and Timoshenko, mentioned in chapter 2. The test is performed as described in
DS/ENôÙ16929. Note that the test is typically used to determine the 1 kN deflection, denoted as
a response parameter, which has shown a good correlation to the vibration serviceability. [27,
11, 10, 53]
The necessary equipment is listed below:
ãÂ CLT panel and base setup as in Section 1.1.2
ãÂ Miras 2, sartorius scale with an uncertainty of 0.005 kg
ãÂ Steel baseplate
ãÂ Magnet with arm
ãÂ Laser displacement sensor, ILD1300ôÙ200
ãÂ Holder for laser displacement sensor
ãÂ Weights of approx. 100 kg with an max load pad area of 200 cm2
The test setup of this experiment can be seen in Figure B.2.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
50
==PAGE:69==
Figure 1.9: Test setup of the Static Unit Point Load Deflection.
The test procedure is as follows:
Attach the laser holder to the magnet arm and place the magnet on the steel base plate and
turn it on. It should now be secure enough to screw the laser displacement sensor itself to the
holder and move the described setup in position. For a single spanning element with simple
supports, the position is at mid span as the element will experience the largest deflection here.
The laser is connected and configured to a computer and NIôÙFlexLogger by a USB cable.
At the same position on top of the element, a concentrated load will be placed. According
to DS/ENôÙ16929, the concentrated load can be up to 1 kN and must include a load pad of a
maximum area of 200 cm2 and the mass of the load must be measured with an accuracy of
minimum 0.1%. The mass, used in the test, can be seen in Figure 1.10 and it has an area of
approx. 190 mm2, see Appendix C.1 for exact dimensions of the masses. Their total weight is
measured with an accuracy below 0.1%, which fulfills the demand, and the total weight of the
masses was 99.96 kg, which is 0.98 kN (assuming g = 9.81 m/s2).
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
51
==PAGE:70==
Figure 1.10: Masses and scale for Static Unit Point Load Deflection.
To ensure that both the masses and the laser are positioned at midôÙspan, the distance to the
supports is measured and verified to be equal before marking both the top and bottom of the
element. The laser is then adjusted by rotating and vertically moving it to ensure that the meaôÙ
surement is perpendicular to the mark and within the visible measuring range.
The test is ready to begin by logging the signal from the laser before, during and after the load is
applied. The experiment is repeated a total of three times with a minimum of a 1ôÙminute interval
between each run. Note that the positions of the masses and laser are reset after each run,
although it is not recommended by DS/ENôÙ16929 to reset the laser after each run. However,
this is done to ensure that the laser is not mistakenly rotated in all three measurements, which
could result in incorrect measurements. At a later stage, it must be confirmed that the differences
between successive readings are less than 5%, as stipulated by the standard.
Data Processing
The data is extracted from NIôÙFlexLogger and loaded into MATLAB. The script Static_Deflection.m
can be seen in AdditionalAppendix. Firstly, the mean of the voltageôÙsignal measured before and
after applying the load is calculated, respectively. These two mean values are then converted
to distances (in mm) by the sensitivity given in Appendix F.6 as
260 mm ã60 mm
5 V ã1 V
= 50 mm/V
(1.1)
The difference between the distances must be equal to the deflection.
The laserãs sensitivity has also been controlled by a simple background test described in ApôÙ
pendix B.

Data Processing
When the test is done, the measured voltage signals must be converted to the correct format.
The data is extracted from NIôÙFlexLogger into a .csv file and loaded into the MATLAB script in
CorrectFormatOfData.m, AdditionalAppendix. The first step is to transform the .csv file from
being separated by ã,ã to ãëƒã. The second step is to convert the accelerometer data from volts to
SI units (m/s2) by applying the sensitivity from the calibration chart of each sensor, respectively.
Then, the file is converted to a .txt file.
The .txt files, containing the accelerations from each run in a test, can then be combined and
used to identify modal parameters using the openôÙsource Operational Modal Analysis tool: pyôÙ
OMA2. This package is made for the Python programming language and can be run in Spyder,
which is an openôÙsource environment for Python.
The script, used for the Operational Modal Analysis with pyOMA2, can be seen in 1E.py, 1+2E.py
and 1+2+3E.py, AdditionalAppendix. The overall process of the analysis consists of the followôÙ
ing steps.
ãÂ The needed packages are imported.
ãÂ The .txtôÙfiles (containing the measurements of the vibration response) for all runs in a test
are loaded.
ãÂ The multiôÙsetup utilizing the concept of roving and reference sensors is defined by using
Pre Global Estimation ReôÙscaling (PreGER).
ãÂ The data is filtered using detrending and decimation.
ãÂ The geometry of the CLT panel(s) is loaded. This file contains the overall geometry, the
different sensor locations and information on the reference and roving sensors. The files
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
55
==PAGE:74==
are Geo_1Element.xlsx, Geo_2Elementer.xlsx and Geo_3Elementer.xlsx, AdditionalApôÙ
pendix.
ãÂ The algorithms, FDD and SSIôÙCOV, used to operate the modal analysis are loaded.
ãÂ The FDD is specified to display the SVD plot for a nxseg of 1024 and the natural frequency
of every peak is defined in a ãnatural frequency vectorã.
ãÂ The vector is utilized as an input for SSIôÙCOV, which is specified to generate modal properôÙ
ties of mode shapes and damping ratios along with the stabilization diagram. An iteration
of specifying ãblock rowsã, ãordermaxã and ãorderã is performed in order to extract the
modal properties from the stable poles. This is because the natural frequencies are not
necessarily displayed in the diagram nor the most stable for a given set of parameters.
1.5
Resonance Tests
The following three experiments, referred to as ãSweepã, ãAccelerationã and ãDamping Ratio,ã
are conducted consecutively. First, the ãSweepã experiment is performed to determine the natôÙ
ural frequency for a specific mode shape with the highest possible accuracy. The obtained
natural frequency is then used in the subsequent ãAccelerationã experiment, which aims to estiôÙ
mate the accelerations in the CLT floor when it is excited at resonance for that particular mode.
Finally, the ãDamping Ratioã experiment is conducted to determine the damping ratio for the
specific natural frequency/mode shape. All of these experiments share the common feature
that the CLT floor is excited at resonance using a shaker with the reaction mass assembly (see
Section 1.1.4).
The natural frequencies and associated mode shapes to be examined include the 1st bending
mode, 1st torsion mode and 2nd bending mode. This analysis is conducted for a single CLT
panel, two CLT panels connected by varying screw spacings, and three CLT panels connected
by varying screw spacings. To excite each of the aforementioned mode shapes, the shaker
must be positioned at the location where the largest deflection for each mode occurs. AdditionôÙ
ally, accelerometers will be used in these experiments to measure the accelerations of the CLT
panel(s). The accelerometers are strategically placed to capture the largest possible acceleraôÙ
tions in the CLT floor, excluding the location where the exciter is positioned. Efforts have also
been made to place the accelerometers in such a way that they capture part of the deflected
shape.
The locations of the sensors and the exciter can be seen in the figures below for the different
modes Figure 1.12 for test of a single panel. Locations for ãconnected panelsã tests can be
seen in Figure C.2 and Figure C.3 in Appendix C.2

a sineôÙwave at a specified frequency. Additionally, the power amplifier is used to adjust the
magnitude of the amplitude of the reaction masses. This setup allows for manual control over
the acceleration of the reaction masses and consequently the force delivered by the shaker,
though with some uncertainty.

1.5.1
Sweep
The purpose of the following experiment is to determine the natural frequency of the CLT panel(s)
when exciting it with a shaker. The exciterãs frequency is manually tuned, in order to specify the
frequency of which the largest amplitude is found, based on acceleration measurements of the
sensors. The found frequency is equal to the natural frequency of the system. The frequency
from the ãRandom Vibration with MultiôÙsetupã is utilized as an initial guess before tuning the
exciter.
The base setup with mounting clips is required and the equipment for data acquisition is set
up as described in Section 1.1.4. Additionally, the setup for the shaker is set up as explained
above in Section 1.5.
The shaker and accelerometers are positioned according to the number of elements and the
mode shape, as shown in Figure 1.12, Figure C.2, and Figure C.3. In NIôÙFlexlogger, the acôÙ
celerometers expected to experience the highest accelerations, are monitored in real time. The
initial guess of natural frequency, found in the ãRandom Vibration with MultiôÙsetupã experiment,
is utilized to perform the initial tuneôÙin (Step 1 in Table 1.8). Within a ôÝ10 Hz range of the iniôÙ
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
59
==PAGE:78==
tial guess, the signal generator frequency is gradually increased by 1 Hz at a time allowing
the CLT panel to reach a steadyôÙstate response. This is to account for the added mass of the
exciter, which lowers the actual frequency compared to the initial guess. At each increment,
the acceleration response is observed. The natural frequency is identified at the peak accelôÙ
eration value, i.e. when the accelerations increase and then decrease. This is utilized as an
initial guess for another tuneôÙin. A total of two more tuneôÙins are performed with the interval
and increment according to Table 1.8 to estimate the natural frequency of the system. For each
frequency increment, the accelerations from all accelerometers are recorded in NIôÙFlexlogger
for approximately 6ã7 seconds after the CLT panel(s) has reached a steadyôÙstate response.

Data Processing
The recorded accelerations at each frequency during the step 3 (FineôÙTunning) are utilized to
determine the natural frequency, which corresponds to the frequency at which the CLT panel(s)
experiences the largest accelerations.
To achieve this, a MATLAB script, SineSweep.m (see Additional Appendix), has been develôÙ
oped. The script loads the recorded acceleration data and converts the units from V to m/s2. It
then analyzes each of the 10 accelerometers attached to the CLT floor individually. For each acôÙ
celerometer, the script calculates the mean acceleration value at each frequency. These mean
values are subsequently plotted in a (frequency, acceleration) plot, as shown in Figure 1.14.
This plot allows for the identification of the natural frequency at which the accelerometers regisôÙ
ter the highest accelerations.
In some cases, it is observed that the different sensors register their largest mean acceleration
at different frequencies. In such instances, the sensor positioned closest to the location of maxiôÙ
mum deflection in the mode shape is selected for analysis. This location generally corresponds
to where the largest accelerations occur.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors

==PAGE:79==
Figure 1.14: Example of plot regarding the ãSweep experimentã for one element, 1E, excited
in 1st mode shape. It can be observed that 10.82 Hz induces that largest accelerations in the
floor and is hereby chosen as the natural frequency.
1.5.2
Acceleration
The purpose of the following test is to estimate the magnitude of the accelerations of the CLT
panel(s) when the three mode shapes, previously described, are excited at their respective natuôÙ
ral frequencies as determined in the ãSweepã experiment. These accelerations will be estimated
for three different force levels applied by the exciter.
As with the ãSweepã experiment, the base setup, data acquisition equipment, and shaker conôÙ
figuration remain consistent. The shaker and accelerometers are positioned according to the
number of elements and the mode shape, as outlined in Figure 1.12, Figure C.2, and Figure C.3.
The signal generator is set to the specific natural frequency corresponding to the mode shape.
The accelerometer attached to the shakerãs reaction mass is monitored in real time using NIôÙ
Flexlogger, while the power amplifier is adjusted until the reaction masses achieve an accelerôÙ
ation of 2.5m/s2. Once this target acceleration is reached, the system is allowed to stabilize.
Through live monitoring of the accelerometers attached to the CLT panel, it can be ensured that
the floor response has reached a steady state. Upon stabilization, NIôÙFlexlogger is activated to
record the accelerations of the CLT floor (and the shaker) for 30 seconds.
The same procedure is then repeated for shakerôÙreactionôÙmass accelerations of 1.25m/s2 and
0.6m/s2, respectively.
Data Processing
The accelerations, recorded during the experiment, are imported into MATLAB using the script
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
61
==PAGE:80==
Acc_....m, AdditionalAppendix for data analysis. The analysis begins by converting the accelôÙ
erations from volts to m/s2. Next, the mean value of the largest accelerations (the amplitude
of accelerations) for each sensor is calculated. These values are then normalized by the mean
accelerations recorded for the reaction masses of the shaker. This normalization allows the
accelerations in the CLT panel to be expressed relative to the excitation force. Such normalôÙ
ization is necessary because it is not feasible to precisely tune the power amplifier during the
experiment to apply the exact same excitation force each time. Through this procedure, the
accelerations experienced by the CLT panel at resonance are estimated.
1.5.3
Damping Ratio
The purpose of this experiment is to estimate the damping ratio associated with the three preôÙ
viously identified natural frequencies, using a shaker, and measure the free decay of the CLT
panel. The damping ratio is determined for three different excitation forces applied by the shaker
in order to record the freeôÙdecay from different amplitudes.
As for the ãSweepã experiment the base setup, data acquisition equipment and shaker configôÙ
uration is kept the same. The shaker and accelerometers are placed in their correct locations
depending on the number of elements and mode shape as described in Figure 1.12, Figure C.2
and in Figure C.3.
The signal generator is set to the specific natural frequency associated with the mode shape.
The accelerometer, attached to the CLT panel and expected to experience the largest accelôÙ
erations, is monitored in realôÙtime using NIôÙFlexlogger. The power amplifier is then adjusted
until the accelerations in the selected accelerometer reach the specified values, as shown in TaôÙ
ble 1.9, depending on the test. The specified values were determined based on the capabilities
of the available equipment.

The system exhibits oscillations before settling to its final equilibrium position. Therefore the
logarithmic decrement method is applied to estimate the damping ratio. This is explained in
Section 3.2.3.
The damping ratio is estimated for all sensors for both n = 1 and n = 3 in each test. Specifically,
for n = 1, all damping ratios obtained in a single test are compiled into an (a[m/s2], öÑ[%])ôÙplot.
This enables an analysis of the damping ratioãs dependence on the acceleration amplitude,
facilitating the identification of the type of damping experienced by the CLT panel.
Furthermore, the damping ratios are calculated as itemized below, considering that the positive
and negative peaks may not be symmetrically distributed around zero. This asymmetry can
result not only from sensor offset but also from differences in the magnitudes of the positive and
negative accelerations.
ãÂ Logarithmic decrement is based on the positive peaks (only)
ãÂ Logarithmic decrement is based on the absolute value of the negative peaks (only)
ãÂ Logarithmic decrement is based on a mean value of the positive peak and absolute value
of the corresponding negative peak

2
Finite Element Method
The main purpose of the numerical investigation is to be able to shed light and compare the
modal properties of a single element and multiple connected elements to equivalent monolithic
elements along with their vibration response in regards to a Foot Fall Analysis.
It is required for the software conducting the investigation to be able to calculate the eigenfreôÙ
quencies, mode shapes and include ãinôÙhouseã Foot Fall Analysis and it needs to include either
programmability or short setup and calculation time.
Generally, most newer literature including a numerical investigation utilize the software, ABAQUS.
([29, 36, 13]. It is typical chosen and known for its robustness and includes many features, but it
lacks the ãinôÙhouseã Foot Fall Analysis and can in some cases include a relatively longer setupôÙ
time than other programs. The authors decided on the choice of FEMôÙDesign23 by StruSoft, a
structural analysis software, for this project, as it fulfills the requirements.
2.1
Modelling of Experimental Setup and Eigenfrequencies
The modeling and calculation of modal properties is made within the 3D Structure Application
and is described for a single CLT element. It also applies for a monolithic element due to their
similarities.
A timberôÙplate under the ãShellã tab in the header of ãStructureã is selected. Click on the ãDefault
settingsã. Go to ãMaterialã and change the mechanical model from ãOrthotropic shellã to ãCross
laminated timberã. The product library is then automatically updated with CLT producers. The
specific product equal to our test panels is found and chosen (BBS XL 200). Shear coupling
between the layers is considered, as there is glue between the layers. No glue at narrow sides
is not considered as it is already included for Ey = 0. Note, that there also is an option to modify
the material. This is described and utilized later in Section 2.2
Table 2.1: Stiffness reduction factors (row, col) in plate stiffness matrix (adapted from FEMôÙ
design).
Parameter
Drilling
Membrane
Shear
Notation
r33
r66
r77
r88
According to DIN EN 1995ôÙ1ôÙ1 r66 = 0.25, when there is no glue at narrow sides of the lamellas.
The same result can be obtained by including Ey = 0, which is the case for BBS XL 200. In
general it means that the shear stiffness in plane is reduced as there is no glue at narrow sides
between the lamellas.
The specific coefficients of the plate stiffness matrix can be seen in in the properties of the plate
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
65
==PAGE:84==
within ãMaterialã after the product has been chosen. Click ãDisplay stiffnessã in the bottom left
corner.
The plate dimensions can then be defined in the 3DôÙmodel view. The length of a plate is modôÙ
eling as equal to the effective span of the element. The support area of 75 mm at each end of
the experimental base setup is therefore neglected and one element is modeled with the dimenôÙ
sions 0.62x5.85 m equal to the standard dimensions of the panel (the thickness is included in
the material choice). For multiple combined elements, the single element is copied and placed
with one adjacent edge to the original element.
Boundary conditions are added in the header of ãStructureã by going to the ãSupportã tab and
the ãLine supportsã. Line supports are added along the ends of the element and its stiffness is
assumed to be ãinfinitelyã stiff in the zôÙdirection (vertical) and zero in all other directions in both
compression and tension. The same goes for the end connection of the element to the support.
This is due to the experimental setup not including a connection between the elements and the
UNPôÙ200 steel beams.
Go to header ãLoadsã and add a new load case with type ãOrdinaryã, duration class is not imporôÙ
tant for this project. The load case should include the dead load of the specific element, which
is determined in General Testing Section 1.2. The load is applied as a surface force in the Force
tab. In addition, the factor within the Dynamic load tab and ãLoad case ôÙ mass conversionã is
set equal to 1 for the dead load load case. This is in order for the modal mass of a given natural
frequency to be related to the total mass in the case of mode shape normalization.
Go the header ãFinite elementsã and choose a size of 0.02 m for ãAverage surface element sizeã
under the ãSizeã tab. FEMôÙDesign includes an automatic finite element size optionëƒ however,
it was not utilized to maintain manual control over the analyses. The chosen element size is
chosen on the background of a convergence analysis of a single element seen in Section D.6.1.
A choice of 0.02 m was found appropriate in regards to run time.
Go the header ãAnalysisã and perform a calculation within ãCalculateã (CalculatorôÙicon). Choose
ãEigenfrequenciesã under ãDynamic analysisã. Add an appropriate number of shapes to capture
all modes within 100 Hz. Remove the 90% horizontal effective mass. Mode shape normalization
to unity. Consider masses in ZôÙdirection (vertical) only and click OK to start the calculation.
Thereby, only the eigenfrequencies, and associated mode shapes and modal masses in the
ZôÙdirection are analyzed for. The calculation is based on the free, undamped EVP and the
equation is defined equal to the Eq. 3.15 in Section 3.2. It is solved by Lanzcos Method in the
utilized 3D module. [54]
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors

==PAGE:85==
Lanczos Method was initially developed by Cornelius Lanczos [55] and has been further
developed due to its numerical instability e.g. in Ojalvo and Newman, 1970 [56] and
updated to be more efficient e.g. in Jonas and Patrick, 1989, [57]. The generalized EVP
is written as
Ax = ö£x
(2.1)
The method reduces the n x n symmetric matrix, A into a tridiagonal matrix, T, by a series
of orthonormal vectors which belongs to the Krylov subspace. The tridiagonal matrix is
seen to be created by a pattern, called the threeôÙterm recurrence relation, which can be
easily computed by an algorithm, which the method utilizes. The eigenvalue problem is
written as
Tjs = ö¡s
(2.2)
where ö¡ in certain cases are named ãRitzã values and the associated eigenvectors are
named ãRitzã vectors. The eigenvalues of T approximates the eigenvalues of A.
ãQuick toolsã should now open automatically, when the calculation is done. Go to ãResultsã,
ãEigenfrequenciesã, ãVibration shapesã, ãTranslational displacementsã in order to see the numôÙ
ber of modes, frequency, period and associated modal mass. A mode no. is displayed by
clicking the mode no. or modal properties, and clicking display. The mode can be animated
ãAnimate resultã in ãManage Resultã in the ãAnalysisã header.
2.2
Finite Element Model Updating
The finite element method (FEM) is based on a deterministic finite element model updating
(FEMU) approach utilizing the natural frequencies determined by the experimental investigation
ãRandom Vibration with MultiôÙsetupã as reference for the finite element models. The specific
approach and stepôÙwise procedure is seen below
1. Sensitivity analyses of the mechanical parameters
2. Calibration of ãdetermined sensitiveã mechanical parameters for single elements (E1, E2
and E3)
3. Sensitivity analyses of the panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection parameters
4. Utilization of the calibrated single elements to connect and calibrate 2E (E1+E2) and 3E
(E1+E2+E3) with the specific screw spacings with ãdetermined sensitiveã panelôÙtoôÙpanel
connection parameters
2.2.1
Mechanical Parameters
The mechanical standard properties of BBS XL 200 in FEMôÙdesign are seen in Figure 2.1. It
includes the layer configuration regarding material, thickness, angle of layer, the modulus of
elasticity parallel and perpendicular to the grain, Poissonãs ratio, shear modulus in xyôÙ, xzôÙ and
yzôÙplane and density of each layer.

2.2.2
Connection Parameters
There are three different methods to model the connection between multiple elements and they
are denoted by ãPanelã, ãEdgeã and ãPointã. ãPanelã and ãEdgeã are both applying line stiffness,
whereas ãPointã are applying point stiffness at userôÙspecified locations. ãPanelã connection can
only be assessed when the analytical model is set to a panel within the general setup.
ãPanelã is by far the quickest model to setup and is exploited on elements while assuming the
same mechanical properties for each element in a floor configuration. It is expected that the
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
68
==PAGE:87==
method is the most utilized in the industry, as the mechanical properties generally will be asôÙ
sumed constant and equal to the standard properties given by the producer.
ãEdgeã and ãPointã can be applied for elements with different mechanical properties and is exôÙ
pected to include a better performance in regards to the calibration of a FEôÙmodel.
All methods includes multiple stiffness parameters, which is listed below (the friction factor is
not relevant). FEMôÙdesign utilizes the local coordinate system for the stiffness. The stiffness
therefore include (ã) next to the specific axis.
