Additionally, a sensitivity analysis is conducted on specific layer parameters. The layôÙ
ers are numbered from the top of the element, with L1, L2, and L3 being analyzed. L4 is
assumed to have the same influence as L2, and L5 is considered equivalent in influence
to L1. It is based on the same single panel model as step 1, however changing ôÝ10%
from the initial standard property given by the producer for each specific layer for each
parameter.
3. Calibration 1E
The natural frequencies determined in the experimental investigation is utilized to caliôÙ
brate the mechanical parameters of each panel (E1, E2 and E3) by running the ãEigenôÙ
frequenciesã analysis. The aim is to include the lowest deviation in a general, but focusing
especially on the lower modesëƒ 1st bending and 1st torsion modes. The most sensitive
parameters from step 2 is utilized and the parameters are changed for all layers at the
time also equal to step 2.
4. Connection Parameters
Sensitivity analyses of the connection parameters stated in Table 2.5 are performed first.
5. Stiffness Variation
This step involves configuring a twoôÙpanel model with standard properties, incorporating
the ãPanelã connection parameters, and applying a surface load of 0.87 kN/m2. Initially,
the connection is assumed to be rigid, with the specified stiffness values applied to all
parameters. Subsequently, the connection is considered fully free, with zero stiffness in
all directions. To assess the influence of individual parameters on the eigenfrequencies,
each parameter is sequentially activated to a fully rigid state while all others remain free.
6. Specific Stiffness
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors

sensitivity analysis to shed light on their combined influence at specific setôÙvalues for both

7. Calibration 2E
The connection sensitivity analysis is utilized to setôÙup new models with the ãEdgeã and

mechanical parameters from step 3. The aim is the same as step 3 also ôÙ calibration in

for all screw spacings.
8. Calibration 3E
Identical as step 7 but in regards to three panels.
Additionally, to the above procedure and schematic overviewëƒ the calibrated 2E and 3E eigenfreôÙ
quencies for all screw spacings are compared to a ãPanelã connection including the calibrated
ãEdgeã connection and equivalent monolithic floor based on the mean mechanical parameters
of the panels at hand and the standard properties from the producer.

2.3.1
Excitation Methods
This section includes the description of the excitation methods of ãSelf excitationã, and ãFull exciôÙ

The ãSelf excitationã and ãFull excitationã are two very similar methods. Though, ãSelfã calculates
the response at the load position node whereas ãFullã calculates the response everywhere else
than the load position node.

eigenfrequencyã determines which eigenfrequencies should be included in the response sumôÙ

1
Experimental Tests
This chapter includes the results and a discussion of the experimental tests. Especially, obôÙ
served tendencies and uncertainties are deliberated. Furthermore, the strengths and weakôÙ
nesses of the utilized methods are addressed. Additionally, the uncertainties associated with
the installation of the buttôÙjoint connection will be assessed.
The mean value of the maximum acceleration, measured in a sensor, is referred to as accelerôÙ
ation in this chapter.
1.1
General Testing
The general tests consist of measurements of element dimensions, element mass, element
moisture content, and the temperature and humidity in the room.
The temperature and humidity in the room were measured multiple times over the course of the
experimental testing.
Figure 1.1: Temperature and humidity according to specific dates.
It can be seen that the temperature is relatively steady around approx. 20.6ãÎC with a deviation
of ôÝ 0.3ãÎC. However, it was observed that the humidity in the room decreased by approximately
30% from the beginning to the end of the experiments. This is more than likely due to the outside
temperatures decreasing during the testing period (as we reached winter months) leading to
lower indoor humidity levels.
Wood is a natural material and therefore the humidity influences the element properties of the
CLT elements. As a consequence of the decreasing humidity during the testing period, the
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
76
==PAGE:95==
moisture content in the CLT elements is also decreasing, see Figure 1.2. This happens as the
CLT elements are trying to reach a state of equilibrium with the surrounding conditions.
Figure 1.2: Water content in the elements according to specific dates
It should be noted that the CLT elements are relatively dry when the elements are brought inside
the testing room as they have been securely stored in a container where they have not been
exposed to all kinds of wind and weather.
Since the decrease in the room humidity and moisture content of the CLT elements is observed,
the mass and dimensions of the elements are measured before and after the testing period.

the actual moisture content in all layers of the CLT panels.

that this scale has not been calibrated within the required time limit and is therefore technically
not valid. Nonetheless, this pallet scale is used daily in the laboratory, and the measured masses
are generally within the range specified by the manufacturer. As a result, the pallet scale is
assumed to provide sufficient accuracy for this experiment.

1.3
Random Vibration with MultiôÙsetup
The ãRandom Vibration with MultiôÙsetupã experiment estimates the natural frequencies, mode
shapes, and damping ratios of 1ôÙ2ôÙ3 panel compositions with different screw spacing. Initially,
the natural frequencies are discussed followed by the associated mode shapes, and the dampôÙ
ing ratios are considered last.
1.3.1
Natural Frequencies and Mode Shapes
Natural frequencies below 100 Hz were estimated from the random vibration test analyzed
with pyOMA2 with the associated mode shapes identified. Modes are classified based on the
number of half sine waves in the longitudinal (orthogonal to the line supports) and transverse
(parallel to the line supports) directions. The modes that are only experiencing half sine waves
in the longitudinal direction will be referred to as bending modes, while the modes that also
experience half sine waves in the transverse direction (a transverse bending mode, combined
with torsion) are referred to as torsion modes. As an example (2,1) is the second bending mode
in the longitudinal direction, while (1,2) relates to the first torsion mode.
The detected natural frequencies up to 100 Hz can be seen in Table 1.3 for one panel, two
connected panels and three connected panels including the screw spacing, respectively. This
table is referenced throughout the current subsection. Note that the modes are ordered in a way
where they follow an ascending order of the natural frequencies for the floor composed of three
connected panels. This means that natural frequencies for a single element and two connected
elements are necessarily not presented in their ascending order.

For the single element, it can be seen that five natural frequencies have been identified below
100 Hz. The mode shapes associated with these natural frequencies can be seen in Figure 1.3.
It is evident that the fundamental frequency corresponds to the first bending mode. Furthermore,
according to dynamic theory for beams, the three bending modes (1,1), (2,1), and (3,1) and their
associated natural frequencies follow an ascending order, corresponding to an increase in wave
number. This relationship holds true, regardless of the number of connected panels, as shown
in the table.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
80
==PAGE:99==
Figure 1.3: Mode shapes visualized with pyOMA2 for a single element.
It is observed in Table 1.3 that for the first two bending modes, the natural frequencies remain
largely constant with only slight deviations, regardless of the number of connected elements.
This behavior aligns with the expectations based on the bending theory of beams, as described
in . The material and mechanical properties of the connected panels are essentially identical
and consequently, the stiffness and mass per unit length of the floor increase proportionally. As
a result, the ratio between stiffness and mass remains unchanged, yielding the same natural
frequency as for a single panel. The two connected panels may therefore be assumed to act
as an equivalent monolithic panel in regard to the bending modes.
For E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3, the third bending mode was difficult to estimate due to closely
spaced modes. Based on the expected bending mode behavior, mode (3,1) is assumed to be
at the lower end of 80 Hz, regardless of the number of connected panels. This is supported by
the data consistently showing a response in this range despite the mode shape visualization
not being distinct enough for definitive identification. Similarly for a single panel, modes (2,1)
and (1,2) are closely spaced at 4ä0 Hz and 4ä4 Hz, which make them hard to distinguish using
pyOMA2. Given the expected bending mode behavior, mode (2,1) is assigned to the consistent
4ä0 Hz frequency observed for E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
81
==PAGE:100==
For E1, E2 and E3 in mode (1,1), a deviation in the natural frequency of E1 is observed relative to
E2 and E3. This discrepancy is likely due to the placement of the panels in relation to the hybrid
frame. E1 was positioned at the center of the supports, whereas E2 and E3 were placed on
either side of E1, bringing them closer to the support edges. The stiffening elements, consisting
of 8û8 lumber, were located at the edges of the supports, potentially increasing the stiffness
experienced by E2 and E3 compared to E1. This difference in stiffness may have influenced
their modal properties.
Across all torsion modes, the natural frequencies are seen to generally decrease as additional
elements are connected from a single panel to E1+E2 to E1+E2+E3. As this is observed, it is
expected that an increasing number of complex torsion modes emerge below 100 Hz for E1+E2
and E1+E2+E3, respectively. The new torsion modes could likely have been identified above
100 Hz initially. Mode (1,2) and additional torsion modes that appear in the threeôÙpanel configôÙ
uration, compared to the singleôÙpanel case, are illustrated in Figure 1.4. Unlike the ascending
order of bending modes, there is no clear pattern for the torsion modes. However in general,
modes with more half sine waves tend to occur at higher frequencies.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
82
==PAGE:101==
Figure 1.4: Selected mode shapes for three connected elements visualized with pyOMA2.
Modes (2,3) and (2,4) are presented from the side and front, as the mode shapes viewed from
an angle are too complex to interpret clearly.
The decrease in natural frequency for torsion modes with an increasing number of connected
elements could likely be attributed to the coupling of these modes. For example, mode (2,2)
involves both half sine waves in the longitudinal direction and opposing torsions around the
longitudinal axis, which is likely to lead to significant mass participation in both directions during
vibration. As panels are added, the systemãs width increases, causing torsions to experience a
longer distance from the edge (where maximum deflection occurs) to the axis of rotation. This
increases mass activation, resulting in a corresponding decrease in the natural frequency.
Examination of modes (1,2) and (2,2) in E1+E2 in the table above, the natural frequency gradôÙ
ually increases as the screw spacing is reduced. This trend persists across all torsion modes
in E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3. This could be explained by the increased screw density enhancing
the connection stiffness, thereby increasing the natural frequency. While some estimation inacôÙ
curacies must be considered, the consistency of this pattern across all torsion modes supports
its validity. The magnitude of frequency variation differs among modes, with some exhibiting
minor changes and others spanning a broader range.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
83
==PAGE:102==
It is observed that the locations of the screws relative to the deflected shape of the torsion modes
appear to be relevant. For mode (1,2) in E1+E2, a c/c distance of 3000 mm (one screw in the
middle) significantly affects the natural frequency compared to a single element. Reducing the
c/c to 2400 mm (adding two screws near the supports) results in only a 0.4 Hz increase. Further
reducing the c/c to 1200 mm (adding screws at the quarter points) increases the frequency by
0.8 Hz, and afterwards decreasing screw spacing yields only minor changes. A similar trend is
observed for mode (2,2) in E1+E2 with the most significant increase in natural frequency of 5.5
Hz when c/c is reduced from 2400 mm to 1200 mm.
This tendency can be explained by the fact that when two individual panels are connected by
a single screw (c/c 3000 mm), their individual deflection shapes are significantly restrained,
though not completely. Adding screws closer to the supports (c/c 2400 mm) does not result in a
significant impact, as the panels are already essentially undeflected at the supports. However,
when screws are placed at the quarter points, they optimally enhance the stiffness of the conôÙ
nection at these locations, thereby significantly restraining individual movement. Consequently,
this screw placement promotes a more monolithic behavior of the panels. Further addition of
screws beyond this point does not result in a substantial effect.
It can be observed in some mode shapes that they are not purely a unique deflected shape. For
example in Figure 1.3, mode (1,2) clearly exhibits one half sine wave in the longitudinal direction
at the edges and two half sine waves in the transverse direction, as expected. However, in the
middle of the panel along the longitudinal direction, two half sine waves are visible, whereas
the axis should remain undeflected for a pure (1,2) mode. This suggests that the mode may be
a linear combination of multiple modes. Alternatively, this trend could result from inaccuracies
during the laboratory work or the analysis phase, particularly when dealing with closely spaced
modes. In contrast considering mode (1,2) for three connected elements in Figure 1.4, the
middle longitudinal axis appears more or less undeflected.

in Figure 1.5.

In general, all estimated damping ratios range from a minimum of 0.1% to a maximum of 8.5%,
with most falling within the interval of 0.5% to 2.5%. This aligns well with the modal damping
ratio of 1% assumed for timber floors in 1995ôÙ1ôÙ1:FU:2020, and 2.5% in prEN:1995ôÙ1ôÙ1. For
certain modes, the damping ratio interval is wider, while for others, the deviation is minimal.
It is difficult to depict any clear pattern in the damping ratios with respect to modes or the number
of connected elements, as the estimates are highly scattered. This is expected, as accurately
estimating damping ratios through Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) is more challenging than
estimating natural frequencies. One contributing factor is the difficulty in applying robust signal
processing techniques to address random and bias errors in the signal. Additionally, OMA
assumes that the input force is random and uncorrelated, an assumption that is challenging to
meet, particularly in realôÙlife scenarios where the input force is often beyond control. [60]
In the SSIôÙCOV technique, the number of block rows has a significant impact on the interval
of damping ratios and can also influence whether a damping ratio is estimated at all. This is
because if a natural frequency cannot be estimated with the specified number of block rows,
the corresponding damping ratio cannot be estimated either. The block row number and order,
used to estimate the damping ratios and natural frequencies, are provided in Appendix D.2.
Experimental and Analysis Method
Overall, a random vibration test in combination with Operational Modal Analysis (OMA) is a
highly effective, important and valuable tool. This method has proven to be of great signifôÙ
icance in this project, as it was essential to estimate the modal parameters, including natural
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
86
==PAGE:105==
frequencies, mode shapes and damping ratios, for both individual and connected CLT elements.
Several factors can influence the measurements in a random vibration test. Overall, the estiôÙ
mated natural frequencies and mode shapes are relatively accurateëƒ however, certain aspects
could have been improved to yield even more precise estimates.
In the laboratory, it was often necessary to walk on the 8x8 lumber connected to the support and
walk on the CLT elements that were not included in the testing. This was due to the width of the
elements, which in some cases made it impossible to reach the entire surface without walking
on the setup. This likely influenced the results, as it was evident that larger vibrations (and
even smaller ones) propagated through the support from the tested CLT panel (see Appendix
B). Therefore, it can be assumed that the vibrations caused by walking on the nonôÙtested CLT
panels also transmitted through the support to the tested panel. The walking was conducted
carefully to minimize this effect.
The accelerometers used in the experiment are crucial for data collection. While minor noise
is typically observed in the accelerometers, additional noise from surrounding laboratory tests
can be captured (see Appendix B), potentially disturbing the measurements.
The use of a bar clamp to excite the structure proved effective in this case. Different tools were
tested (see Appendix B), but it can happen that other tools that are even heavier could have
yielded better results. A limitation of the used approach with a clamp is that the person applying
the excitation force must be able to manage the weight of the tool. Additionally, the clampãs
limited length was one of the reasons, why walking on the setup was necessary. This is an
important consideration, particularly when dealing with wider panels, as it may become difficult
to cover the entire surface during excitation. In general, the testing procedure was not found to
be ergonomically satisfactory.
When transitioning from the experimental measurements to the analysis phase, additional paôÙ
rameters influence the estimation of the modal parameters.
Various techniques exist for conducting Operational Modal Analysis (OMA). This project comôÙ
bined Frequency Domain Decomposition (FDD) and CovarianceôÙdriven Stochastic Subspace
Identification (SSIôÙCOV) to improve validation of the estimated modal parameters. This apôÙ
proach helped distinguish closely spaced modes and reduce spurious modes, which is a comôÙ
mon challenge in OMA. Using two techniques enabled comparison and ensured result consisôÙ
tency.
Closely spaced mode shapes were encountered for a single element, as discussed previously.
During the OMA analysis, several natural frequencies were observed to correspond to the same
mode. In such cases, further investigation of these modes was necessary.
Another challenge for pyOMA2 in estimating modal parameters arose when elements were conôÙ
nected solely by frictionôÙpulled together with a clamp and then released. This scenario, included
in the ãTesting Programã (Table 1.1) but not in the presented results, led to poorly detected mode
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
87
==PAGE:106==
shapes and closely spaced natural frequency peaks, making it difficult to distinguish real from
spurious frequencies. Since the panels were not fully connected, their individual natural freôÙ
quencies likely remained distinct yet closely spaced due to their nearôÙidentical properties. The
stabilization diagram for the friction test of E1+E2 is shown in Appendix D.3.
A significant challenge in distinguishing modes using FDD was the use of the PreGER MultiôÙ
Setup in pyOMA2 to combine datasets. This approach resulted in only a single SVD line apôÙ
pearing in the SVD plot, likely representing an averaged SVD from all datasets. Consequently,
the underlying SVD lines, which are typically available and aid in identifying modesãparticularly
closely spaced onesãwere not visible. This absence complicated the peakôÙpicking process, as
illustrated in Figure 1.6, which shows an SVD plot for test 1+2+3E with c/c 1200 mm.
Figure 1.6: SVD plot for 1+2+3E c/c 1200mm.
This issue is possibly due to a lack of familiarity with the coding framework in the pyOMA2
toolbox, as only a limited number of examples are provided by the developer. However, since
the toolbox is still under development, it could also be a limitation.
The modal parameters estimated using FDD were compared with those determined by SSIôÙCOV,
which produces a stabilization diagram for analysis. This diagram was essential for assessing
the validity of identified modes, particularly because the number of block rows (br) and the model
order could be adjusted. By evaluating different br and order values and observing which natural
frequencies remained consistent, it was possible to determine with greater confidence whether
a mode was real or spurious. The stabilization diagram corresponding to Figure 1.6 is shown
in Figure 1.7. Additionally, SSIôÙCOV was used to extract mode shapes.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
88
==PAGE:107==
Figure 1.7: Stabilization diagram for 1+2+3E c/c 1200mm, br=400, order=200.
The process of manually adjusting br and order values is timeôÙconsuming and relies heavily
on personal judgment and experience, making it challenging to optimize and estimate parameôÙ
ters objectively. Additionally, quantifying the uncertainties and inaccuracies in the estimates is
difficult. Regarding computational efficiency, SSIôÙCOV was preferred over SSIôÙDAT due to its
significantly lower computational time.
In general, it would have been a significant improvement on the aboveôÙmentioned concerns if a
commercial tool as ãARTeMISã had been available. In these kind of programs the optimization
of the estimates is carried out automatically by the program in regard to e.g. MAC values and
other criteria. Furthermore, the analysis can also be carried out while the test is conducted.
Thus, it can be seen immediately if a test needs to be reôÙdone. References have also been
found to utilize the ARTeMIS software for data processing. [33, 13] where the excitation was
ambient or based on a hammer test, respectively.
Another important consideration in the experimental setup is the spacing of the accelerometer
measurement grid. A denser grid allows for more measurement points, increasing the data
available per test and improving the accuracy of the estimates by capturing vibration patterns
more effectively. This is particularly beneficial for enhancing the visualization of mode shapes.
However, a finer grid significantly increases data collection and processing time, especially
when using pyOMA2. Given that the sampling frequency in these tests was set to 1000 Hz,
more than three minutes of acceleration measurements were required each time the sensors
were repositioned.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
89
==PAGE:108==
1.4
Sweep
In this project, the objective was to estimate the damping ratio and vibration response of a CLT
floor for the modes (1,1), (1,2), and (2,1). The damping ratios were initially determined using
Operational Modal Analysis (OMA), with the results presented in Table 1.4. However, since OMA
often struggles to accurately estimate damping ratios, an alternative method was employed to
improve accuracy. By exciting the CLT floor at resonance, the corresponding damping ratio and
vibration response (accelerations) have been determined. To achieve resonance excitation, the
ãexactã natural frequency was identified through an experiment known as ãSweep,ã as described
in Section 1.5.1.
In this experiment, the natural frequencies for modes (1,1), (1,2), and (2,1) have been estimated
and are presented in Table 1.5.
Table 1.5: Natural frequencies of E1, E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3 based on experiment ãSweepã.

The table indicates that the natural frequency of mode (1,1), corresponding to the fundamental
frequency, is slightly lower for E1 compared to E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3. Similarly, the fundamenôÙ
tal frequency of E1+E2 is marginally lower than that of E1+E2+E3. In general, the fundamental
frequency increase for the bending modes as additional panels are connected. This may be
explained by the relative mass of shaker to the total mass of the panel(s).
Additionally for mode (2,1), the natural frequency exhibits a gradual increase as more elements
are connected, a trend that is further reinforced by decreasing the screw spacing.
For the torsion mode (1,2) and bending mode (2,1) in E1, the natural frequencies are closely
spaced. However, as additional elements are connected, the natural frequency of mode (1,2)
decreases significantly. This reduction can be attributed to increased mass participation from
adjacent panels due to the added connections.
In general, the frequencies are seen to increase as the screw spacing for a given setup is
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
90
==PAGE:109==
decreased. This may be due to the higher stiffness of the connection associated with the deôÙ
creasing screw spacing.
Experimental and Analysis method
First and foremost, it would have been highly beneficial to conduct the sweep experiment for
E2 and E3 as individual elements. This would have provided a clearer opportunity to validate,
with greater certainty, whether E2 and E3 exhibit increased stiffness compared to E1, due to
the placement on the support.
In this experiment, the shaker, along with the reaction mass assembly, played a crucial role in
exciting the floor. To amplify the signal from the signal generator to the shaker, a power amplifier
was employed. An analysis of the shaker was conducted with the power amplifier held constant,
while the signal generator was configured to produce frequencies within the following intervals:
[0 : 1 : 35] Hz and [35 : 5 : 100] Hz ôÙ the middle value in the brackets is the increment.
Figure 1.8: Test of the mean max acceleration of the reaction masses at different frequencies.
The analysis, presented in Figure 1.8, reveals that the acceleration of the reaction masses
initially increases significantly up to 4 Hz then decreases substantially until 60 Hz and subseôÙ
quently reaches a near steady state between 60 Hz and 100 Hz.
This suggests that the force (i.e., the acceleration of the reaction masses) from the shaker may
vary slightly across the different frequencies included in a single test during a ãSweepã experôÙ
iment within the lower frequency range. This variation impacts the accelerations of the floor,
making the selection of the frequency with the highest floor acceleration potentially misleading.
This effect is primarily relevant at lower frequencies, as the change in acceleration of the reôÙ
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
91
==PAGE:110==
action masses becomes negligible at higher frequencies. Unfortunately, it was not possible to
mitigate this issue, as the power output of the amplifier could not be precisely adjusted to ensure
a consistent force from the shaker at each frequency.
In certain cases, different sensors indicated that the maximum acceleration occurred at different
frequencies within a single test. This could be due to CLT being composed of wood, a natural
and orthotropic material, which results in local variations across the floor. In these cases, the
sensor located closest to the largest deflection, as predicted by the specific mode shape, was
considered the governing sensor. This variability also introduces uncertainty in the selection of
the correct frequency.
Consequently, it is important to acknowledge that the estimated natural frequencies are subject
to uncertainty due to various factors. In most cases, the uncertainty is ôÝä0.1 Hz, as a distinct
peak in acceleration was clearly observed within this range around the determined frequency.
1.5
Damping Ratios
In this experiment, the damping ratios for a single element (E1), E1+E2, and E1+E2+E3 were
estimated for modes (1,1), (1,2), and (2,1), respectively. This was achieved by exciting the
floor at resonance using a shaker and subsequently turning it off. The damping ratio was then
determined by applying the logarithmic decrement method to the free decay response of the
floor.
Firstly, the potential amplitude dependency of the damping ratio was examined. This was asôÙ
sessed by plotting the damping ratios determined for n=1 (where the damping ratio is calculated
using consecutive peaks) based on their corresponding amplitudes, as shown in Figure 1.9.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
92
==PAGE:111==
Figure 1.9: (Amplitude, Damping Ratio)ôÙgraph for E1, mode (1,1).
In some of these tests, particularly for modes (1,2) and (2,1), large standard deviations were
observed, which will be discussed further below. Due to these deviations, the trend line, used to
check for amplitude dependency, was fitted to the damping ratios there were found in the range
of 0.0ã3.0%. Additionally, damping ratios corresponding to amplitudes below approximately 0.3
m/s2, which are primarily influenced by irregularities and sensor noise, were excluded from the
trend line analysis.
It can be seen for the majority of the tests that the tendency line is constant as seen above
in Figure 1.9, meaning that there is no amplitude dependency. For tests where only small
amplitudes are recorded, the calculated damping ratios are very scattered due to noise which
can lead to the tendency line having an unrealistic incline.
The overall constant tendency lines imply that the damping in the floor is generally from viscous
damping. It is not possible to say if there is any friction damping present for the small amplitudes
as there is very much noise in this area. This would be indicated by a decline of the damping
ratio for increasing amplitude when considering the soôÙcalled fractional powerôÙlaw model. Fluid
damping is assumed to not be relevant in most cases of the tests ôÙ for fluid damping, the damping
ratio would be seen to incline for larger amplitudes. [41].
However, a tendency line with a slight incline for a few tests regarding mode (2,1) for E1 and
E1+E2 (c/c 3000mm, 1200mm and 300mm) is found. The tests all included a high intensity
of the shaker acceleration. This is shown in Figure 1.10. For this specific test, the incline in
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
93
==PAGE:112==
damping ratio was 0.13%/m/s2. This could imply that fluid damping is present. This incline
in damping ratio for larger amplitudes can also be caused by other factors, e.g. that some
of the other modes are activated and influence the damping. This is something that could be
interesting to investigate further.
Figure 1.10: (Amplitude, Damping Ratio)ôÙgraph for E1, mode (2,1)
In the subsequent analysis, it is assumed that all tests exhibit predominantly viscous damping.
Therefore, a mean value of the damping ratios, calculated for each mode and panel (or no. of
connected panels), has been determined. Each mean value of the damping ratios is presented
in Figure 1.11.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors

Figure 1.11: Mean values of Damping ratio in each mode.

fluctuate somewhat for different c/c values within each mode. This variability is likely due to
the inaccuracies observed in the mean values, as shown in Table 1.6. The damping ratios in
Figure 1.11 are determined with n=1.

It is evident that the standard deviation for mode (1,1) is very small independent for both a single
panel and connected panels. In contrast, modes (1,2) and (2,1) exhibit much larger standard
deviations. This is understandable, as higher modes with greater complexity make it more
challenging to estimate an accurate damping ratio. For instance, the free decay observed for
panel combination E1+E2 c/c 300 mm in mode (1,2) (shown in Figure 1.12) demonstrates that
the free decay is not smooth. This could be due to the free decay being influenced by both
mode (1,2) and mode (1,1), as the modal participation factor of mode (1,1) is not zero due to
the position of the shaker.
Figure 1.12: Free decay of E1+E2, c/c 300mm, mode (1,2), sensor location F5.
InTable 1.6 (above), an attempt was made to reduce the standard deviations by increasing the
value of n. For n=3, a reduction in the standard deviation was observed, as expected, since
small inaccuracies between adjacent peaks are likely to be eliminated.
For n=1 and n=3, the mean value of each mode for E1, E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3 was determined
by including all damping ratios across the different c/c. These mean values are presented in
Figure 1.13.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
96
==PAGE:115==
Figure 1.13: Mean damping ratio for each element combination and each mode for n=1 and
n=3.
For n=1, it can be observed that the damping ratios of the floor for the three modes are in the
interval of 0.7% to 1.6%. This clearly indicates that the structure is an underôÙdamped system
(öÑ<1) and it should therefore be emphasized that the estimated natural frequencies above are in
fact, damped natural frequencies according to Eq. 3.27. [41] However, for such small damping
ratios, the difference is negligible.
It can be observed that the damping ratios for modes (1,1) and (1,2) decrease linearly as more
elements are connected. Mode (1,2) generally exhibits the highest damping ratio, while the
damping ratio for mode (1,1) is approximately 0.6% lower than that of mode (1,2). The damping
ratio for mode (1,1) remains around 0.8% across the different panel combinations. This reducôÙ
tion in damping for modes (1,1) and (1,2) was anticipated, as it can likely be attributed to the
significant increase in mass when panels are connected. According to theory, an increase in
mass leads to a decrease in damping [41].
In contrast for mode (2,1), the damping ratio shows the opposite trend, increasing as more eleôÙ
ments are connected. Initially, the damping ratio for mode (2,1) is the lowest among the three
modes for E1. However, it steadily increases and nearly reaches the same damping ratio as
mode (1,2) in the E1+E2+E3 configuration.
In Weckendorf et al. 2008 [61], an experimental evaluation of damping ratios in fullôÙscale timber
flooring systems for various modes was conducted, examining how damping varied under difôÙ
ferent dead weights. The study observed a decrease in damping ratios for the first and second
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
97
==PAGE:116==
modes as dead weight increased, while the third mode exhibited an increase in damping ratio
with higher dead weight. Although the experiments in [61] involved a timber flooring system
with varying dead weights, and this project focuses on connected CLT panels, the results are
not directly comparable. However, it is noteworthy that, in this project, the damping ratio for the
third mode also increases as more panels are connected. This can be interpreted as a parallel
to the increase in floor mass, as well as an increase in the systemãs width.
For n=3, where smaller standard deviations are observed in Table 1.6, the mean values are very
similar to those obtained for n=1, as seen in Figure 1.13. This suggests that the mean values
derived using n=1 also hold a high degree of validity. A key advantage of using n=1 is that it
allows for the determination of amplitude dependency in the damping ratio, providing additional
insight into the damping behavior of the structure. However, this cannot be achieved with n=3,
although the accuracy of the damping ratios is improved.
Experimental and Analysis method
As only one shaker was available, modes (1,2) and (2,1) were not ideally excited at resonance.
The shaker was placed in one of the two locations with the largest deflections for these modes.
To ensure complete accuracy and maintain symmetry in these modes, an additional shaker,
operating out of phase with the initial one, should have been positioned at the other deflected
location. Additionally, the shakers should have been connected to the floor in order for the
upward force to be applied properly.
The shaker is quite large and heavy, and since it is placed directly on the CLT floor, it may
influence the floorãs acceleration measurements. It might have been more appropriate to use a
smaller exciter or an advanced one with a thrust linkage to the CLT floor to mitigate the impact
of the added mass.
It should be noted that one of the sensors, used in the experiments, experienced higherôÙthanôÙ
usual noise levels. However, most of this noise was eliminated by applying a threshold to the
values used in the analysis.
The above discussed regarding the sensors and shaker is also valid in following ãAccelerationsã
experiment.
It was observed in some tests that the positive and negative accelerations, measured by a
sensor, were not symmetrical, meaning that the floor accelerated more than it decelerated, or
vice versa. To ensure this did not significantly affect the determined damping ratios, the damping
ratios were calculated using the average of the positive and corresponding negative peaks, as
well as using only the positive or negative peaks from the free decay.
Since the damping ratios calculated from the positive peaks, negative peaks, and average peak
values were all within the same order of magnitude, the damping ratios, presented in this results
section, are based on the average peak values. The damping ratios, determined using only the
positive and negative amplitudes, can be found in Appendix D.4, respectively.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
98
==PAGE:117==
1.6
Accelerations
The aim of the ãAccelerationsã experiment was to estimate the vibration response of the CLT
floor in terms of acceleration when excited at resonance. This was done for a single element
(E1), E1+E2, and E1+E2+E3 for modes (1,1), (1,2), and (2,1), respectively.
The limited number of accelerometers were strategically placed on the CLT floor to optimally
capture the highest acceleration, based on the deflected shape of each mode. This placement
also allowed for the observation of portions of the deflected shape. As shown in Figure 1.14 for
E1+E2, mode (1,1) with c/C 2400 mm, the acceleration is highest at the center of the elements
and decreases as one moves closer to the supports, clearly depicting the first bending mode.
Figure 1.14: Max accelerations in floor normalized with acceleration of shaker in specific sensor
locations. This is specifically for excitation of E1+E2, mode (1,1) with c/c 2400 mm. The blue
square is the shaker location in the specific test.
The highest acceleration in each mode has been determined. In Figure 1.15 for E1+E2, it can be
observed how the highest acceleration in the floor is related to the screw spacing. The normalôÙ
ized floor acceleration remains relatively constant when considering screw spacing, suggesting
that the elements are effectively connected as early as at c/c 3000 mm, which corresponds to
a single screw for E1+E2. During the tests, it was also observed, that with just one screw, the
different elements were moving as one cohesive unit. This could be attributed to the elements
being tightly positioned due to the single screw securing them, as well as the possible friction
between the elements.
The same figures for E1 and E1+E2+E3 can be seen in Appendix D.5.

specific mode and c/c. High, Med, Low are different intensities of ashaker. The text in the plot
specifies the location of the max accleration in the floor.

==PAGE:119==
It is seen for each mode that the intensity of the shaker yields deviations in the afloor,max/ahaker,
and the intensity of the shaker, resulting in the highest floor acceleration, is systematic but
differs in each mode. To provide an overview of the accelerations, a mean value with standard
deviation, incorporating all screw spacings and shaker intensities, has been calculated for each
panel combination. These results are presented in Figure 1.16 below.
Figure 1.16: Mean value of afloor,max/ahaker for E1, E1+E2 and E1+E2+E3 including all screwôÙ
ing spacing and shaker intensity when relevant, respectively. The standard deviation is also
shown.
For a single element, it can be observed that the maximum acceleration in mode (1,1) is approxôÙ
imately 3.5 times higher relative to the shakerãs acceleration. The acceleration in mode (2,1) is
lower than in mode (1,1), while the acceleration in mode (1,2) is nearly the same as the shakerãs
acceleration.
It can be observed that for modes (1,1) and (2,1), the maximum acceleration in the floor deôÙ
creases as more elements are connected. This is expected, as additional mass is added each
time a panel is connected. According to Newtonãs second law, the acceleration should be halved
when the floorãs mass is doubled, assuming the excitation force remains constant. However, this
does not fully hold true in this case. It may be due to E1 and E2 being connected by a buttôÙjointëƒ
the floor is not an ideal unified structure with double the mass of a single element. As a result,
not all of the adjacent panelãs mass may contribute to the systemãs overall mass. Nevertheless,
vibrations are effectively transmitted through the connection, as indicated in Figure 1.16.
Unlike modes (1,1) and (2,1), mode (1,2) is more complex and therefore introduces greater
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
101
==PAGE:120==
complexity in the acceleration distribution across the floor. The maximum acceleration has
been observed for mode (1,2) as shown in Figure 1.17, where the edge opposite to the shaker
position experiences the highest acceleration.
The system experiences angular acceleration due to the moment generated by the excitation
force. Therefore, an increase in the number of connected panels (i.e., an increasing moment
arm) results in an increase in acceleration. Conversely, as more mass is added with each
additional connected panel, the acceleration tends to decrease. These two factors create a
balance between the angular acceleration and the massôÙinduced reduction in acceleration. As
shown in Figure 1.16, the acceleration for mode (1,2) remains relatively constant from E1 to
E1+E2, but it shows a slight increase when the third panel is added. It is important to note that
this mode exhibits the largest standard deviations among all modes.
Figure 1.17: Max accelerations in floor normalized with acceleration of shaker in specific sensor
locations. This is specifically for excitation of E1+E2, mode (1,2) with c/c 2400 mm. The blue
square is the shaker location in the specific test.
ãDamping Ratioã discussed the sensors and shaker in the earlier subsection. This discussion is
also valid in the ãAccelerationã.
Ideally, sensors would have been placed at all locations on the CLT floor during the experiments.
In principle, it is uncertain whether there are unrecorded locations that experience larger accelôÙ
erations than those observed. However, the sensor placements in the various tests were strateôÙ
gically adjusted based on the deflection shape of each mode, with a focus on areas where the
largest accelerations were anticipated.
Additionally, when analyzing the acceleration from the reaction masses, it was occasionally obôÙ
served that the acceleration was slightly larger than the deôÙacceleration, a phenomenon not
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
102
==PAGE:121==
solely attributable to sensor offset. In some cases, the acceleration also exhibited deviations
over time within the same test. Consequently, a mean of the acceleration and deôÙacceleration
has been used in the analysis. This deviation may be due to the sensorãs attachment to the
reaction masses with beeswax. If an excessive amount of beeswax was applied, it could have
created an elastic layer between the reaction masses and the sensor, causing them to move
relative to each other, though minimally.
1.7
Installation of ButtôÙJoints
When connecting the panels using the buttôÙjoint connection, it is to ensure an angle of 45ãÎto
the surface of the CLT floor and ensure that the screws are perpendicular to the connection line.
However, achieving this alignment consistently is challenging. The customôÙmade jig proved to
be highly useful, but in some cases, the drill was observed to slightly deflect at the point of entry.
This issue was likely caused by the insufficient length of the jigãs guiding cylinder not being close
enough to the CLT surface. This distance enhances the lack of stiffness of the drill itself causing
deflections in both upwards and sideward direction in regards to the 45circ line of drilling. This
can be seen in Figure 1.18.
Figure 1.18: Deflection of the preôÙdrill tip slightly changes the angle of entry point and thereby
the line of the screw.
Furthermore a countersink drill was used to facilitate countersinking. This was done manually by
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
103
==PAGE:122==
rule of thumb, resulting in slight variations in countersinking depth for each screw. Efforts were
made to achieve a depth where the screw would be perfectly flush with the CLT floor surfaceëƒ
however, complete uniformity could not be ensured.
It should be noted that in the experiments involving two and three connected panels, a small
fragment of the drill became lodged between E1 and E2 near the support, approximately 5 cm
from the edge. The drill bit broke during the preôÙdrilling process and could not be removed, as
the CLT panels had already been pressed together.

2
Finite Element Modelling
This chapter presents the results derived from the numerical investigation with a particular focus
on observed trends. Additionally, the strengths and limitations of the employed methods are
critically evaluated.
For clarity, the following definitions are provided:
ãÂ ãEdgeã refers to a connection characterized by line stiffness, associated with varying meôÙ
chanical parameters for the included panels.
ãÂ ãPanelã denotes a connection with line stiffness, but governed by a single set of mechanical
parameters for the included panels.
ãÂ ãPointã represents a connection with point stiffness, linked to different mechanical paramôÙ
eters for the included panels.
2.1
Sensitivity Analysis of Mechanical Parameters
A summary of the sensitivity analyses examining the effects of various parameters on the eigenôÙ
frequencies is presented in Table 2.1 and Table 2.2, with comprehensive results available in
Appendix D.6.2.
The initial analysis was performed on a single element, applying an approximate ôÝ10% variaôÙ
tion in mechanical parameters from their standard values across all layers simultaneously. An
exception was made for the modulus of elasticity (E), which was analyzed separately for the
longitudinal and transverse layers. It is important to note that the dead load of the element was
set at 0.87kN/m2 instead of the standard value of 0.94 kN/m2. Deviations from reference were
determined based on eigenfrequencies for modes up to 100 Hz.
Key findings from this sensitivity analysis are summarized in Table 2.1, with the complete analôÙ
ysis detailed in Appendix D.6.3.1. Among the most influential mechanical parameters was the
surface load, specifically the dead load (m). This exhibited a mean change of ôÝ5.2% of the
natural frequencies relative to the reference across all modes. This outcome is consistent with
expectations, as the eigenvalue problem (EVP) is highly sensitive to mass variations.
The Youngãs modulus parallel to the grain in the span direction, ExL, is particularly influential
for the bending modes. This is consistent with expectations, as the apparent bending stiffness
depends on E, and bending mode frequencies are a function of the apparent bending stiffness.
The impact of ExL is greatest on the lowest bending mode and decreases with higher bending
modes.
The transverse shear modulus, Gxy, is influential only for the torsion modes. This is consistent
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
105
==PAGE:124==
with theory, as the torsion modes include distortion in plane due to the angle changes in both
(y,z)ôÙ and (x,z)ôÙplane.
The rolling shear modulus, Gyz, is influential for the first and second torsion modes. This is in
line with expectations as there is a distortion in the (y,z)ôÙplane. Its influence also increases with
higher bending modes.
ExL and Gxy are the parameters that predominantly affect either bending or torsion modes,
respectively. This makes them highly applicable in calibration of the 1E model with varying
mode shapes. Gyz, is incorporated if deemed necessaryëƒ particularly adjusting its value leads
to a reduction in general deviations.
The following deviations are calculated relative to the reference frequencies of approx. 12, 40,
44, 77 and 88 Hz for the mode shapes, respectively.

Foot Fall Analysis
The footfall analyses are based on the calibrated connection parameters from the FEMU. Two
setups including two panelôÙsizes are modelled. The setups are based on the prEN:1995ôÙ1ôÙ1
LFF and HFF definitions with the 8 Hz limit. The panel of 0.62 m BBS XL 200 from the earlier
experimental and numerical investigations is chosen and a 3 m wide BBS XL 200. Both panels
are specified to be equal to standard properties given by the producer to mimic a designerãs
pointôÙofôÙview. The connection method is based on ãPanelã.
Figure 2.11 shows the output from a footfall analysis regarding a maximum response node.
Figure 2.11: Footfall vibration response.
2.6.1
Initial Analyses
The damping ratioãs, öÑ, effect on the response for a LFF regarding its steadyôÙstate resonant
response and a HFF regarding its transient response is shown in Figure 2.12 for the 0.62 m
single element. The response decrease within the given range is approx. a factor of 9 for
the LFF and 1.6 for the HFF. The damping ratio is therefore found to significantly decrease
the resonant response within the lower damping ratios and converges to a constant response
for the larger damping ratios. This tendency is in line with theory as the maximum dynamic
amplification factor, DAF, is inversely proportional to 2öÑ. Increasing damping ratio is found to
decrease the transient response within the interval of 0.5%ôÙ5%. The tendency in the figure
is seen as linear, although the data points has a slight curvature to them. This is in line withëƒ
assuming that a single step from a foot fall is equal to a freeôÙdecay. The freeôÙdecay can be

fitted with the exponential decay model for a given initial amplitude and frequency. For various
damping ratios, the rootôÙmeanôÙsquare of the amplitude is lower for increasing damping ratio,
but it isnãt a ãviolentã decrease. The same tendencies can be seen for 3 m single element in
Appendix D.7.

3
Discussion
This section includes a general discussion of the results and the utilized methods of both the
experimental and numerical investigations.
3.1
Natural Frequencies and Mode Shapes
The natural frequencies of the experimental setups were first determined up to 100 Hz. Random
Vibration tests were performed, during which acceleration data were recorded and analyzed
using Operational Modal Analysis (OMA). This approach facilitated the estimation of modal
properties such as natural frequencies and corresponding mode shapes.
The Sweep test was more timeôÙconsuming and, as a result, was limited to identifying only three
mode shapes: (1,1), (1,2) and (2,1). The analysis was performed for a single CLT panel as
well as for configurations of two and three panels connected by buttôÙjoints, with varying screw
spacing.
The natural frequencies of the three first modes, which are estimated by Random Vibration
(OMA) and Sweep, are presented along with their deviation in Table 3.1. Here, the natural
frequencies obtained from OMA serve as the reference, and the natural frequencies derived
from the Sweep test are compared to these. This is chosen as the shaker introduces additional
mass to the system, potentially affecting the actual natural frequency values. However, this
does not imply that OMA provides entirely accurate values, as there are uncertainties in this
method as well, particularly when dealing with closely spaced modes.

In general, the natural frequencies determined using the Sweep test are lower than those estiôÙ
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
122
==PAGE:141==
mated with OMA. This is likely due to the additional mass introduced from the shaker. However,
similar magnitudes and trends are observed for the two methods.
For both (1,1) and (2,1), the natural frequencies remain relatively constant at 12 Hz and 40 Hz
when elements are connected, with deviations ranging from 0.2 Hz to 1.7 Hz for OMA, respecôÙ
tively. In contrast, the deviations for Sweep range from 0.74 Hz to 3.44 Hz for the same modes.
Overall, the estimated values from the two tests are within the same order of magnitude. This is
consistent with theory, as when connecting the panels the bending stiffness is increased based
on the total width of the structure but equally so is the mass, which yields the same natural
frequency. [41]
Notably, for E1, the natural frequency for (1,2) is higher than that for (2,1) in OMA, whereas the
opposite is observed in Sweep.
For mode (2,1), it is seen that a decrease in natural frequency occurs as elements are conôÙ
nected, but a slight increase in natural frequency for smaller screw spacing. This is valid for
all estimated torsion modes with OMA (see Table 1.3). This tendency of decreasing natural
frequency for torsion modes is also seen in [13, 29], where the modal properties of CLT panels
connected with a halfôÙlapped joint has been investigated. Therefore, the type of panelôÙtoôÙpanel
connection may not have a significant impact on the natural frequencies.
This could be explained by when the elements are being connected the total width of the given
floor is increased, meaning more mass is activated in the transverse direction for the torsion
mode shape, hence the natural frequency should decrease. Additionally, when the screw spacôÙ
ing is decreased the connection stiffness is increased.
Modelling the panels and their connections in FEMôÙDesign also revealed the necessity of inôÙ
creasing the connection stiffness as screw spacing decreased to ensure that the natural freôÙ
quencies of the model and the experiment were consistent for torsion modes. Furthermore, it
was observed that, even with just one screw pair in the experiment, the connection stiffness in
the model corresponded to a semiôÙrigid connection. As screw spacing decreased, the modelled
connection quickly approached a rigid connection. This aligns with what was observed during
the experimentsëƒ even with the largest screwing spacing, the two connected panels were alôÙ
ready moving as one.
When comparing connected panels with an equivalent monolithic floor modelled in FEMôÙDesign,
similar overall trends are observed as in the experimental results for connected panels. AddiôÙ
tionally, the natural frequencies are of the same order of magnitude. However, the equivalent
monolithic floor does not necessarily capture all the complex modes present in the connected
panels. The width of the CLT panels and the number of connected panels influence whether
the connected CLT panels and their equivalent monolithic floor exhibit the same mode shapes.
The Random Vibration test, in combination with OMA, is a highly effective technique for estimatôÙ
ing the natural frequencies of a system. However, it may occasionally produce spurious natural
frequencies, and closely spaced modes can be challenging to differentiate. To mitigate some
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
123
==PAGE:142==
of these issues, various signal processing techniques can be employed. OMA also offers the
advantage of being relatively accessible, as it only requires sensors to measure the systemãs
vibration response, whereas the Sweep test necessitates a highôÙprecision exciter to control the
frequency.
Prior to conducting the Sweep test, performing the Random Vibration test with OMA was both
relevant and valuable. This provided an overview of the modes occurring at specific frequenôÙ
cies, offering a better starting point for the Sweep test. With this knowledge, the shaker could
be placed in the appropriate position to excite the correct mode and at the relevant frequency.
The Sweep test is valuable for verifying whether modes with unclear peaks in the SVD plot are
spurious or real.
3.2
Damping Ratio
Two methods were employed to determine the damping ratio of the floor: Random Vibration
tests with OMA and Resonance with logarithmic decrement method of the free decay. Similar
to the estimated natural frequencies, the damping ratios determined through Resonance were
only obtained for three modes.
In Figure 3.1, the damping ratios, calculated with n=1 of the free decay, are presented. Here, it
is seen that connecting the panels has an influence on the damping ratio, though this is different
for each mode. It was seen that the screw spacing did not have any considerable effect on the
damping ratio and therefore the mean value is presented in Figure 3.1. Overall, the damping
ratios of the three modes in the three element combinations are in the range of 0.7ôÙ1.6 %, which
indicates an underôÙdamped structure.
Additionally, the damping ratio ranges, found with OMA for each experimental setup, were seen
to not be influenced by the screw spacing. Therefore in Figure 3.1, the shown damping ratio
range is based on the maximum and minimum damping ratio found for that element combination.
Figure 3.1: Damping Ratio from Resonance test and Damping Ratio Range from OMA.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
124
==PAGE:143==
In general, the ranges of damping ratio, found with pyOMA2, are relatively reasonable in the
order of magnitude compared to the damping ratio from the logarithmic decrement method.
Though, an exception here is mode (1,2) which ranges from 0.7% to 8.5%.
For mode (1,1) and (1,2), it is seen that the damping ratio is decreasing for additional connected
panels. This aligns well, with the fact that more mass and stiffness is added when an extra panel
is connected.
On the contrary, the damping ratio of mode (2,1) is seen to increase when the number of conôÙ
nected elements increases. A similar trend is also observed in [61], though this research is
based on multiple timber flooring systems, investigating the influence on the damping ratio for
increasing surface mass. When the surface mass of the floor increases, it is seen that the dampôÙ
ing ratio of the two first modes are decreasing but it increases for the third mode. The additional
surface mass might be seen as an analogy to the multiple CLT panels being connected in this
project.
The damping ratio, specified for CLT floors in prEN:1995ôÙ1ôÙ1, is set to 2.5 %. This is approxôÙ
imately twice the damping compared to what is estimated for the CLT panels and connected
panels in this project. The reason for the determined damping ratios being lower than the specôÙ
ification could be due to the fact that as the experiments cannot replicate inôÙsitu conditions in
a laboratory. In [32], it was found that the damping ratio of a CLT floor increased significantly
from 1ôÙ3 % in the laboratory to 6% when fully integrated in the building.
An important point to highlight is that in general uncertainty about the estimated damping ratio
will contribute to more uncertainty about the vibration response of a floor. A high damping
ratio in an underôÙdamped structure will reduce the vibration amplitude, shorten the response
duration and be more prone to mitigating resonance effects. This was also demonstrated in the
initial analysis for a footfall regarding the effect of the damping ratio on a LFF resonant vibration
response. Conversely, a low damping ratio results in prolonged, higherôÙamplitude vibrations
and greater susceptibility to resonance.
It can be observed from the two methods that OMA with SSIôÙCOV is not highly accurate in
estimating the damping ratio, as discussed earlier. An alternative approach would be to explore
other signal processing techniques to estimate the damping ratios, such as EFDD (Enhanced
Frequency Domain Decomposition), to determine if they yield more accurate results.
The logarithmic decrement method provides accurate mean values, and the type of damping
present can be characterized through the tests. Though, it can be quite tedious to pick out the
data points that should be included in the free decay due to noise in the sensors, and the test
itself is timeôÙconsuming.
3.3
Damping Ratio and Vibration Response
The damping ratios, found at resonance with the shaker, and the maximum vibration response,
found at resonance, are given in Figure 3.2 for each element combination for the three first
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
125
==PAGE:144==
modes, respectively.
Figure 3.2: Double axis figure showing the max accelerations found in the floor on the right side
and the damping ratio on the left side for mode (1,1), (1,2) and (2,1).
It can be seen for mode (1,1) that the maximum acceleration decrease for decreasing damping
ratio when more elements are connected. This can be caused by the extra added mass being
more governing on the vibration response compared to the damping ratio.
For mode (1,2) the max acceleration increase slightly for decreasing damping ratio when more
elements are connected. Here the decrease in damping ratio and the longer moment arm from
the excitation force must be slightly more governing compared to the increase in mass.
At last for mode (2,1), the accelerations are decreasing for increasing damping ratio. As exôÙ
plained above, a higher damping ratio results in lowerôÙamplitude vibrations which fits with the
development of mode (2,1). Furthermore, the extra added mass also influences the acceleraôÙ
tions to decrease.
Overall, it is found that the buttôÙjoint connection between CLT panels does influence the modal
properties and vibration response of the CLT floor. The influence cannot be entirely attributed
to the connection itself, but rather to the changes in width, mass and stiffness that occur when
connecting the panels.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
126
==PAGE:145==
3.4
FEM and FEMU
The CLT floor and panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection are modelled in FEMôÙDesignôÙ23 with a determinôÙ
istic FEMU method based on the following analyses and calibrations in the order of
1. Sensitivity analyses for mechanical parameters
2. Calibration of mechanical parameters for single elements with experimentally determined
eigenfrequencies
3. Sensitivity analyses of connection parameters
4. Calibration of connections between the calibrated single elements for the multiple (2 and
3) connected panel setup with different connection unit spacing based on experimentally
determined eigenfrequencies
Similar literature [13] has based the FEM on an extensive probabilistic FEMUôÙprogram and the
connection, halfôÙlap joint, was modelled as an ãelastic stripã. [36]. The probabilistic approach
is more advanced, timeôÙconsuming to set up, requires more computational effort and applies
probabilistic distributions of parameters to include the uncertainty throughout the analysis. The
uncertainty is especially of high interest. However, since this project placed significant emphasis
on the experimental investigation of the effects of a buttôÙjoint connection, the FEM analyses
and, consequently, the FEMU was timeôÙconstrained. Therefore, the deterministic approach
was deemed more relevant.
This project applied the relevant line stiffness of vertical stiffness Kzãý and rotation line stiffness
around the connection line, Cxãý. The most significant was the vertical stiffness ranging from
5125ôÙ107kN/m/m for different spacings and no. of connected elements. A very little rotational
stiffness range from 0ôÙ1.1 kNm/m/ãÎwas applied in FEMôÙDesign in order to minimize the eigenôÙ
frequencies deviations to experimental data. This was also found by [13], who found that rotaôÙ
tional stiffness, determined from static 4PBT, may not be applied in modal analysis. In general,
it seems that there is a significant difference in connection stiffness for a static and dynamic
case. Additionally, the vertical stiffness had a tendency to be closer to rigid. This is also in line
with the fact that many of the results yielded that the connected elements had close to equal
frequencies of the equivalent monolithic panel.
The connection parameters are calibrated to the eigenfrequencies only, thus neglecting any
mode shape differences. Applying a relatively low vertical stiffness yielded unconnected mode
shapes across the connection line for torsion modes, which is practically not possible. This sugôÙ
gests that FEMU should not solely rely on calibrating eigenfrequencies but should also consider
mode shapes and potentially other parameters.
The natural frequency of the torsion modes (X,2) was initially assumed to decrease from 1E to
2E. However, it is seen that the outôÙofôÙphase bending modes from the unconnected 2E setup
transitioned into torsion modes as the vertical line connection stiffness increased.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
127
==PAGE:146==
ãÂ (1,1) outôÙofôÙphase transitions into (1,2)
ãÂ (2,1) outôÙofôÙphase transitions into (2,2) ... etc.
This phenomenon can possibly be explained as follows: A vertical constraint at the connection
forces the outôÙofôÙphase bending modes to be linked along the connection line. Consequently,
this results in halfôÙsine waves along the outer edges of the panels and a rotational motion around
the connection line.
3.5
Foot Fall
The footfall analysis is based on the selfôÙexcitation method of a single walking person with a
1.6ôÙ2.4 Hz walking frequency and three harmonics according to the DS/EN1991ôÙ1ôÙ1:DKNA. A
lowôÙ and highôÙfrequency floor is modelled using standard properties with the element width, 0.62
m, from the experiment and 3 m panels for 1E, 2E and 3E cases with the calibrated connection
stiffness from the FEMUôÙmethod from earlier. The 3 m wide element is regarded as the approxôÙ
imate maximum limit for transportation and production reasons, which is why it is included.
For the single LFF element of 0.62 m, the maximum acceleration (rms) was determined to
2.41 m/s2 while for the 3 m wide element, it was (0.55 m/s2). This underlines the importance
of utilizing as wide elements as possible in a design when the connection is not taken into
account. An additional reduction of 31% was found (0.55 ã0.38 m/s2 (rms)) from a single 3
m wide element to a 3x3 m wide element setup accounting for the connection. The equivalent
monolithic panel had a reduction of 40%. In general, the connected elements yielded results
similar to what was found for a monolithic panel. This was expected as their eigenfrequencies
are close to equal. The same was found for the HFF with a smaller reduction of 23% and 24%
for the same situations.
It is critical to assess the width and the connection of the panels as they influence the arms and
the number of modes, that occur within the frequency limit of 8 Hz for the LFF and 24 Hz for
the HFF, which should be accounted for. A purely numerical investigation [12] based an elastic
strip [36] and the rotation stiffness of a 4PBT of the investigated connection [37] also yielded
large reduction of the acceleration from a single panel to connected panels.
Design methods of prEN:1995ôÙ1ôÙ1 and Canadian CLT Handbook typically establish frequency
limits based on the first bending mode to define and verify according to a LFF or HFF. When acôÙ
counting for connected panels, the eigenfrequencies of the torsion modes have been observed
to decrease as the number of adjacent panels increases. Consequently, it may be possible
to design spanning floors where the first bending mode suggests a highôÙfrequency floor (HFF),
necessitating a transient response verification according to the floor vibration criteria. In conôÙ
trast, the torsion modes may be within the frequency limits for a LFF, indicating the need for a
resonant response verification. This indicates that more research should be conducted on the
panelôÙtoôÙpanel connectionãs influence on the modal properties and vibration response.
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
128
==PAGE:147==
4
Conclusion
The initial aim of this study was to investigate the influence of the buttôÙjoint panelôÙtoôÙpanel conôÙ
nection and its screw spacing on the modal properties and vibration response of crossôÙlaminated
floors (CLT).
This was investigated experimentally with random vibration tests and resonance tests performed
on a single CLT panel as well as on configurations with two and three connected CLT panels
with the buttôÙjoint, each with varying screw spacing.
The results indicated that the fundamental frequency for all panel configurations was approxiôÙ
mately 12 Hz. The natural frequencies associated with bending modes remained unaffected by
the connection of multiple panels. Conversely, the torsion modes were significantly influenced
by the connection. As the number of connected panels increased, the natural frequencies of the
torsion modes exhibited a substantial decrease. However, reducing the screw spacing along
the connection line led to a slight increase in the natural frequencies of the torsion modes. This
suggests that more complex torsion modes emerge at progressively lower frequencies as addiôÙ
tional panels are connected.
The estimated damping ratios for the first three modes across different panel configurations
were generally within the range of 0.7ã1.6%. Among the three modes, mode (1,2) exhibited
the highest damping ratio. The relation between the damping ratio and vibration response for
increasing connected panels showed that the maximum acceleration of the floor for mode (1,1)
decreased, and so did the damping ratio. For mode (1,2), the acceleration increased slightly
while the damping ratio decreased. For mode (2,1), the acceleration decreased while the dampôÙ
ing ratio increased. Conclusively, the damping ratios and vibration responses were affected by
the connection of multiple panels, though variations in screw spacing did not exhibit a significant
influence.
Furthermore, the project aimed to develop finite element models to assess and compare the
modal properties of a single CLT panel, multiple panels with a panelôÙtoôÙpanel connection and
equivalent monolithic floors, as well as to evaluate their vibration responses under footfall exciôÙ
tation.
A deterministic Finite Element Model Updating (FEMU) approach was employed to incorporate
panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections in modal assessments. Based on experimentally determined natuôÙ
ral frequencies from three 0.62 m wide panels, the methodology involved calibrating mechaniôÙ
cal parameters of individual elements before assembling configurations and refining connection
stiffness parameters.
The calibrated connection stiffness are the vertical line stiffness Kzãý and the rotational line stiffôÙ
ness Cxãý. Kzãý appears to be effectively rigid for nearly all screw spacings at and below 1200 mm,
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
129
==PAGE:148==
whereas Cxãý appears effectively free for all screw spacings. The rigid vertical connection results
in modal properties of connected panels nearly matching those of equivalent monolithic floors.
Neither the monolithic nor singleôÙelement models capture higherôÙorder torsion modes observed
in connected panels, with single panels exhibiting significantly higher torsionôÙfrequencies overôÙ
all. Additionally, the outôÙofôÙphase bending modes (X,1) in an unconnected twoôÙpanel setup
transition into a torsion mode (X,2), as vertical stiffness increases.
The calibrated connection model was further used to examine its impact on vibration responses
in two floor configurations: a lowôÙfrequency (7 m span) and highôÙfrequency (5.85 m span) floor,
both subjected to excitation from a singleôÙpersonãs footfall. Analyses were conducted for 0.62
m and 3 m wide panels.
Results show that acceleration decreases with additional panel connections. From a single to
a threeôÙpanel setup, the acceleration (arms) is reduced by 64% (LFF) and 42% (HFF) for the
0.62 m panel and by 31% (LFF) and 23% (HFF) for the 3 m panel.
Overall, it is found that the buttôÙjoint connection between CLT panels does influence the modal
properties and vibration response of the CLT floor. The influence cannot be entirely attributed
to the connection itself, but rather to the changes in width, mass and stiffness that occur when
connecting the panels.
4.1
Further Work
This project has provided valuable insights into the effect of panelôÙtoôÙpanel connections on the
modal properties and vibration response of CLT floors. However, several aspects warrant furôÙ
ther exploration. This section briefly discusses different methodological considerations and
outlines potential directions for future research.
Several methodological aspects should be noted. Scratching the CLT floor was neither timeôÙ
efficient nor ergonomically optimal. Previous studies [13, 33] indicate that hammer testing proôÙ
vides reliable results, making it worthwhile to explore its applicability in future studies with OMA.
For FEMU, a probabilistic approach [13] could improve parameter calibration, while automating
computational analysis and result extraction would enhance efficiency and accuracy.
Additionally, testing a monolithic floor would allow direct comparison with connected panels and
validation of FEM results. A footfall analysis, incorporating alternative walking models, could furôÙ
ther refine the assessment of footfallôÙinduced vibrations. Additionally, an experimental footfall
analysis could have been performed to verify footfallôÙinduced vibrations from simulations.
Furthermore, two aspects of the damping ratio warrant further investigation. First, the damping
is generally higher for torsion modes. Second, the damping ratio of the second bending mode
increases as more panels are connected. These findings suggest the need for further research
to better understand the underlying mechanisms.
Moreover, the data analysis in this study highlights the importance of assessing torsion modes
when multiple panels are connected, as their natural frequencies decrease accordingly. This
consideration is also relevant for increasing panel widths. Comfort criteria are typically based
Effect of PanelôÙToôÙPanel Connection on Modal Properties and Vibration Response of CLT Floors
130
==PAGE:149==
on the first bending modeëƒ however, it would be valuable to investigate under which panel conôÙ
figurations, torsion modes may become more critical than the first bending mode.
Design guidelines and code provisions are derived floor vibration criteria from extensive datasets
obtained through onôÙsite testing, which measure modal properties, vibration response and subôÙ
jective comfort quantification [19, 18, 17, 2, 22, 7, 62]. Consequently, conducting similar testôÙ
ing is essential to provide additional data that can support the development of the current apôÙ
proaches. An extensive standardized data collection methodology for the human perception
and comfort is especially of interest [53], along with openôÙsource data accessibility. This would
enable broader utilization and facilitate a more comprehensive understanding of comfort across
various applications.

Gamma
ãÂ This method only provides a closed (exact) solution for the differential equation: simply
supported beams or panels with a sinusoidal load distributionëƒ however, the differences
between the exact solution and those for uniformly distributed loads or point loads are
minimal and are therefore acceptable in engineering practice (Ceccotti, 2003).
ãÂ This method ignores the influence of shear deformations in the longitudinal layers on the
total deflection of the panel.
ãÂ The (EI)eff depends on the span length and, thus, is a systemôÙdependent value.
ãÂ Most importantly, this method will not apply to CLT panels with a total of seven or more
layers, which requires some modifications that render it cumbersome.
Extended Gamma
ãÂ It shares similar limitations to the Gamma Method, except for its applicability to CLT panels
with more than five layers (more than three longitudinal layers).
